
These outputs exactly match the given sample output, confirming that the plan produces correct results for the test cases.

---

**Plan Evaluation**

The simulation shows that the proposed algorithm yields the expected answers for all sample cases. The reasoning behind the formula (`answer = (2n)! / 2`) is sound: the number of ascents in a permutation of length `2n` follows the Eulerian distribution, which is symmetric, so exactly half of all permutations have at least `n` ascents.

**Result:** **No Need to Modify Plan**.
**Solution Explanation**

For a permutation `p` of length `2n` define an *ascent* as an index `i (1 ≤ i < 2n)` with `p_i < p_{i+1}`.
There are `2n‑1` adjacent pairs, therefore each permutation has exactly

```
ascents + descents = 2n‑1 .
```

If we reverse a permutation, every ascent becomes a descent and vice‑versa, so the
reversal gives a bijection between permutations with `k` ascents and permutations
with `2n‑1‑k` ascents.  
Consequently the numbers of permutations with `k` ascents and with `2n‑1‑k`
ascents are equal.

`2n‑1` is odd, thus the two groups

```
{ ascents ≥ n }   and   { ascents ≤ n‑1 }
```

have the same size and together contain all ` (2n)! ` permutations.
Hence

```
answer = (2n)! / 2   (mod M) ,   M = 1 000 000 007 .
```

The division by 2 is performed by multiplying with the modular inverse of 2,
`inv2 = (M+1)//2`.

The only work is to compute factorials modulo `M` up to `2·max(n)` once,
then answer each test case in O(1).

---

#### Algorithm
```
read t
read all n_i, keep max_n
limit = 2 * max_n
fact[0] = 1
for i = 1 .. limit:
        fact[i] = fact[i-1] * i mod M
inv2 = (M + 1) // 2          # modular inverse of 2

for each n:
        ans = fact[2*n] * inv2 mod M
        output ans
```

---

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs the required count for every test case.

*Lemma 1*  
For any permutation `p` of length `2n`, let `a(p)` be its number of ascents.
The reversal `rev(p)` satisfies `a(rev(p)) = (2n‑1) – a(p)`.

*Proof.*  
Consider an adjacent pair `(p_i , p_{i+1})`. In `rev(p)` the same two values appear
as `(p_{i+1} , p_i)` but at positions `2n‑i` and `2n‑i+1`.  
If `p_i < p_{i+1}` (an ascent) then `p_{i+1} > p_i` (a descent) in the reversed
pair, and vice‑versa. Thus each ascent of `p` becomes a descent of `rev(p)` and
each descent becomes an ascent. There are `2n‑1` pairs, so the claim follows. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
The number of permutations of length `2n` with at least `n` ascents equals the
number with at most `n‑1` ascents.

*Proof.*  
Define a mapping `f` that sends a permutation to its reversal.
By Lemma&nbsp;1, `a(f(p)) = (2n‑1) – a(p)`.  
If `a(p) ≥ n` then `a(f(p)) ≤ (2n‑1) – n = n‑1`, and conversely.
`f` is a bijection, therefore the two sets have the same cardinality. ∎



*Lemma 3*  
The total number of permutations of length `2n` is `(2n)!`.

*Proof.* Trivial combinatorial fact. ∎



*Theorem*  
For every `n ≥ 1` the algorithm outputs the number of permutations of length
`2n` whose number of ascents is at least `n`, modulo `M`.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma&nbsp;2 the desired set and its complement (ascents ≤ `n‑1`) have equal
size, and together they form all permutations (Lemma&nbsp;3).  
Hence the required count is exactly half of `(2n)!`.  
The algorithm computes `(2n)!` modulo `M` as `fact[2*n]` and multiplies by the
modular inverse of 2, which is equivalent to division by 2 in the field
`ℤ_M`. Therefore the produced value equals the desired count modulo `M`. ∎



---

#### Complexity Analysis  

Pre‑computation up to `2·max_n` takes `O(max_n)` time and memory.  
Each test case is answered in `O(1)` time.  
Given `∑ n ≤ 10^5`, the total time is `O(10^5)` and memory `O(10^5)`.

---

#### Reference Implementation (Python 3)

```python
import sys

MOD = 1_000_000_007

def main():
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    ns = list(map(int, data[1:1 + t]))
    max_n = max(ns)
    limit = 2 * max_n

    fact = [1] * (limit + 1)
    for i in range(1, limit + 1):
        fact[i] = fact[i - 1] * i % MOD

    inv2 = (MOD + 1) // 2  # modular inverse of 2 because MOD is prime

    out_lines = []
    for n in ns:
        ans = fact[2 * n] * inv2 % MOD
        out_lines.append(str(ans))

    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms to
the required input‑output format.

### Problem Understanding  

Luntik has three types of songs:

* `a` songs of length **1 minute**  
* `b` songs of length **2 minutes**  
* `c` songs of length **3 minutes**

All songs must be split into **two concerts**; each song belongs to exactly one concert.  
For a given test case we need the smallest possible absolute difference between the total durations of the two concerts.

Formally, let  

```
S = 1·a + 2·b + 3·c          (total minutes of all songs)
```

If the first concert gets a total of `x` minutes, the second gets `S‑x`.  
The goal is to minimise `| (S‑x) – x | = | S – 2·x |`.  
Thus we need a value `x` that can be formed by selecting some numbers of 1‑, 2‑, and 3‑minute songs and is as close as possible to `S/2`.

The constraints are huge (`a, b, c ≤ 10^9`), so we cannot enumerate subsets. We must rely on number‑theoretic observations that reduce the problem to a constant‑time answer per test case.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (Subset Sum with Small Weights, but Large Counts)**  

> You are given `p` items of weight 1, `q` items of weight 2 and `r` items of weight 5.  
> Find whether it is possible to obtain a total weight exactly equal to `W`.  
> `p, q, r ≤ 10^9`, `W ≤ 10^12`.

**Algorithm Sketch**  

1. Compute the total weight `T = 1·p + 2·q + 5·r`.  
2. If `W > T` → impossible.  
3. Reduce the problem modulo the greatest common divisor of the weights (here `gcd(1,2,5)=1` → no reduction).  
4. Because the weights are small, we can try all possibilities for the number of 5‑weight items that could be used: at most `min(r, W//5)`.  
5. For each candidate `k` (number of 5‑weight items), the remaining weight to achieve is `W' = W – 5·k`.  
6. Now we have only 1‑ and 2‑weight items. The classic condition for representing `W'` with 1‑ and 2‑weights is: `W' ≤ 1·p + 2·q` **and** `W'` is not larger than the maximum possible even/odd parity that can be formed (i.e., if `W'` is odd we need at least one 1‑weight item).  

**Step‑by‑step Python‑like Pseudocode (no actual code)**  

```
def can_make(p, q, r, W):
    total = p + 2*q + 5*r
    if W > total: return False
    max_fives = min(r, W // 5)
    for k in range(max_fives + 1):
        remaining = W - 5*k
        # need remaining <= p + 2*q
        if remaining > p + 2*q: continue
        # parity check
        if remaining % 2 == 0:
            # we can always use 2‑weight items, fill the rest with 1‑weight if needed
            return True
        else:
            if p >= 1:   # need at least one 1‑weight to make odd sum
                return True
    return False
```

**Discussion of the algorithm**  

* The key observation is that the number of distinct weights is tiny, so we can iterate over the count of the *largest* weight (here 5) – at most `W/5` iterations, which is feasible because `W` is at most `10^12` → about `2·10^11` worst case, still too large.  
* However, with the extra insight that the counts are huge, we can bound the loop by the **minimum** of `r` and `W//5`. In many test cases `r` is small, making the loop cheap.  
* For the remaining two weights (1 and 2) we have a closed‑form condition based on parity and total capacity, eliminating any further loops.  

**Planning to solve the example problem**  

1. Read input.  
2. For each test case compute `total`.  
3. If `W > total` → answer “NO”.  
4. Determine `max_fives = min(r, W // 5)`.  
5. Loop `k` from `0` to `max_fives`.  
6. For each `k`, compute `remaining = W - 5*k`.  
7. Check capacity `remaining ≤ p + 2*q`.  
8. Check parity: if `remaining` is odd, ensure `p ≥ 1`.  
9. If both hold → answer “YES”.  
10. If loop finishes → answer “NO”.  

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

Because the song lengths are only **1, 2, 3**, the total sum  

```
S = a + 2·b + 3·c
```

We need a subset sum `x` (duration of the first concert) that is as close as possible to `S/2`.  
The minimal possible difference is  

```
diff = min_{x achievable} |S - 2·x|
```

**Key observations**

1. **Parity matters** – the difference can only be `0` or `1`.  
   * If `S` is even, the ideal target is `S/2`. If we can reach exactly `S/2`, the answer is `0`.  
   * If `S` is odd, the best we can do is `|S - 2·⌊S/2⌋| = 1` (or `|S - 2·⌈S/2⌉| = 1`). So the answer is either `0` (if we can achieve the floor/ceil) or `1`.  
   Hence the answer is never larger than `1`.

2. **When is a difference of 0 possible?**  
   We need to be able to select songs whose total duration equals `S/2`.  
   Because the song lengths are small, we can look at the problem modulo `2`.  
   * A 1‑minute song contributes `1 (mod 2)`.  
   * A 2‑minute song contributes `0 (mod 2)`.  
   * A 3‑minute song contributes `1 (mod 2)`.  
   Therefore the parity of any subset sum is determined solely by the number of **odd‑length** songs (1‑ and 3‑minute).  

   If `S` is even, we need a subset with **even** total. That is always possible unless we have **exactly one odd‑length song** in total (i.e., `a + c = 1`). In that case any subset containing that song is odd, any subset without it is even but then the other concert gets the odd song, making the difference `1`.  

   However, there is a more precise condition discovered in editorial solutions:  
   * If `a + c` is **even**, we can always balance to `0`.  
   * If `a + c` is **odd**, the minimal difference is `1`.  

   This works because we can always move one 1‑minute song to the other side to adjust the parity, unless we have no 1‑minute songs to shift.  

3. **Edge cases with small counts**  
   When `a` is `0` and `c` is odd, we cannot change parity using a 1‑minute song, but we can use a 3‑minute song together with a 2‑minute song (since `3 + 2 = 5` which is odd+even = odd). The full analysis shows that the answer depends only on the parity of `a + c`.  

4. **Result**  
   * If `(a + c) % 2 == 0` → answer `0`.  
   * Else → answer `1`.  

   This matches the sample:  

   * `1 1 1` → `a + c = 2` (even) → `0`.  
   * `2 1 3` → `a + c = 5` (odd) → `1`.  
   * `5 5 5` → `a + c = 10` (even) → `0`.  
   * `1 1 2` → `a + c = 3` (odd) → `1`.  

**Why the answer never exceeds 1?**  
Because the smallest song length is 1 minute, we can always shift a single song from one concert to the other, changing the difference by at most 1 minute. Hence the optimal difference is either `0` (perfect balance) or `1` (off by one minute).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Plan – Step‑by‑step solution for the original problem  

1. **Read input**  
   * Read integer `t` – number of test cases.  
   * Loop `t` times, reading three integers `a, b, c` for each case.

2. **Compute parity of odd‑length songs**  
   * `odd_cnt = a + c` (both 1‑ and 3‑minute songs are odd).  

3. **Determine minimal difference**  
   * If `odd_cnt % 2 == 0` → `ans = 0`.  
   * Else → `ans = 1`.

4. **Output**  
   * Print `ans` on its own line for each test case.

5. **Complexity analysis**  
   * Time: `O(t)` – constant work per test case.  
   * Memory: `O(1)` besides input storage.

6. **Implementation notes**  
   * Use Python’s fast I/O (`sys.stdin.read` or `sys.stdin.buffer.readline`).  
   * Convert the whole input to a list of integers and iterate.  
   * No need to compute the total sum `S` or to handle large numbers beyond 64‑bit, because Python integers are arbitrary precision.

Following this plan will produce a correct, optimal solution that runs comfortably within the limits for all allowed input sizes.
**### Simulation**

Sample input  

```
4
1 1 1
2 1 3
5 5 5
1 1 2
```

Process each test case:

1. `a=1, b=1, c=1` → `odd_cnt = a + c = 2` → even → answer `0`.
2. `a=2, b=1, c=3` → `odd_cnt = 2 + 3 = 5` → odd → answer `1`.
3. `a=5, b=5, c=5` → `odd_cnt = 5 + 5 = 10` → even → answer `0`.
4. `a=1, b=1, c=2` → `odd_cnt = 1 + 2 = 3` → odd → answer `1`.

Collected outputs:

```
0
1
0
1
```

These exactly match the expected sample output.

**### Plan Evaluation**

The plan correctly yields the minimal possible difference for all test cases, including the sample. No issues were found.

**Result:** **No Need to Modify Plan**.
```python
import sys

def solve():
    data = list(map(int, sys.stdin.buffer.read().split()))
    t = data[0]
    out_lines = []
    idx = 1
    for _ in range(t):
        a, b, c = data[idx], data[idx+1], data[idx+2]
        idx += 3
        out_lines.append('0' if ((a + c) & 1) == 0 else '1')
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

completed 36/165, Solved: True, number of success = 36/36, acc = 100.0

### Problem Understanding  

We are given an array `a` of `n (≥3)` distinct positive integers.  
For each test case we must choose a **largest possible** subset of indices whose element‑sum is **composite** (i.e. not prime and greater than 1).  
If several subsets of maximal size exist, any one may be output.  
The statement guarantees that at least one non‑empty subset with a composite sum exists, so a solution is always possible.

Observations that simplify the task  

1. **Whole array first** – Compute the sum `S` of all `n` elements.  
   * If `S` is composite, the answer is simply the whole set (size `n`).  

2. **When `S` is prime** – We need to discard as few elements as possible to make the remaining sum composite.  
   * Removing a single element `a_i` changes the sum to `S - a_i`.  
   * Because all numbers are distinct and `n ≥ 3`, there will always be at least one element whose removal yields a composite sum.  
   * If removing any single element still leaves a prime (or 1), we can remove two elements; the resulting size will be `n‑2`, which is still maximal because we cannot keep all `n` elements.

3. **Parity insight** – Any even number greater than 2 is composite.  
   * If `S` is even and `S > 2`, it is automatically composite.  
   * If `S` is odd (hence possibly prime), removing an odd element makes the new sum even, and if that even sum is >2 it is composite.  
   * Therefore, when `S` is prime (and odd), it suffices to remove any **odd** element. If the array contains at least one odd element, we are done with size `n‑1`.  
   * If all elements are even, `S` would be even, contradicting the “prime” case (the only even prime is 2, impossible because sum of ≥3 distinct positive integers ≥ 1+2+3 = 6). Hence the “prime sum” case always has at least one odd element to drop.

Thus the solution reduces to:

* Compute total sum `S`.  
* If `S` is composite → output all indices.  
* Else (`S` is prime) → find an index `i` such that `a_i` is odd; output all indices except `i`.  

The remaining sum will be even and ≥ 4, therefore composite.  

The only remaining technical piece is a fast primality test for numbers up to the maximum possible sum:  
`n ≤ 100`, each `a_i ≤ 200` → `S ≤ 100·200 = 20000`.  
A simple trial division up to `sqrt(S)` (≈ 141) is more than enough and runs in negligible time for `t ≤ 100`.

---

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (Distinct Subset with Prime Sum)**  
Given an array of distinct positive integers, find a subset of maximum size whose sum is **prime**.  

**Description**  
The task mirrors the original problem but asks for a prime sum instead of a composite one. The approach is similar: start with the whole array, check if its sum is prime, otherwise try to remove the smallest possible number of elements to obtain a prime sum.

**Step‑by‑step solution outline (no code)**  

1. **Compute total sum `S`.**  
2. **Prime test** – If `S` is prime, answer is the whole array.  
3. **If `S` is composite** –  
   * Try removing a single element `a_i`. For each `i`, compute `S - a_i` and test primality.  
   * The first removal that yields a prime sum gives a subset of size `n‑1`.  
   * If none works, try removing two elements (nested loops) to obtain size `n‑2`.  
   * Because `n` is small (≤100) this brute‑force search is acceptable.  

**Algorithm discussion**  
* The primality test is identical to the one used for the original problem.  
* The search order (single removal before double removal) guarantees maximal size.  
* Complexity: `O(n * sqrt(maxSum))` for the single‑removal phase, plus `O(n² * sqrt(maxSum))` worst‑case for double removal – still fine for the given limits.

**Planning for the example problem**  

1. Read input.  
2. For each test case:  
   a. Compute `S`.  
   b. If `S` is prime → output all indices.  
   c. Else iterate over indices, test `S - a_i`. If prime → output all except `i`.  
   d. If still not found, iterate over all unordered pairs `(i, j)`, test `S - a_i - a_j`. If prime → output all except `i, j`.  
3. Print results in the required format.

---

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

1. **Read the test case.**  
2. **Calculate total sum `S`.**  
3. **Composite test** –  
   * If `S` is **not prime** (i.e. composite) → answer is the whole set (`size = n`, indices `1 … n`).  
4. **Prime sum case** –  
   * Scan the array to locate any **odd** element (its index `odd_idx`).  
   * Remove that element: the remaining sum is `S - a[odd_idx]`, which is even and ≥ 4, therefore composite.  
   * Output size `n‑1` and all indices except `odd_idx`.  

**Why this works**  

* An even number larger than 2 is always composite.  
* When `S` is prime, it must be odd (the only even prime is 2, impossible for `n ≥ 3`).  
* Removing an odd element from an odd sum yields an even sum.  
* The sum after removal is at least `S - max(a_i)`. Since `S ≥ 1+2+3 = 6` and we remove at most 199, the resulting even sum is still ≥ 4, guaranteeing compositeness.  

**Primality test** – trial division up to `√S`.  

**Complexities**  

* Time per test case: `O(n + √S)` → ≤ `O(100 + 150)` → trivial.  
* Memory: `O(n)` for storing the array and indices.

---

### Plan (step‑by‑step)  

1. **Setup**  
   * Prepare a helper function `is_prime(x)` that returns `True` if `x` is prime, `False` otherwise, using trial division up to `int(sqrt(x))`.  
   * Remember that `1` is not prime and not composite; however the sum will never be 1 because `n ≥ 3` and all numbers are positive.

2. **Processing each test case**  
   a. Read `n`.  
   b. Read the list `a` of length `n`.  
   c. Compute `total = sum(a)`.  

3. **Check compositeness of the whole sum**  
   * Call `is_prime(total)`.  
   * If it returns `False` (i.e., `total` is composite) →  
        - Print `n`.  
        - Print the indices `1 2 … n`.  
        - Continue to the next test case.  

4. **When the total sum is prime**  
   a. Iterate over the array with enumeration to find the first index `i` where `a[i]` is odd (`a[i] % 2 == 1`).  
   b. Because the sum is odd, such an odd element must exist.  
   c. Build the list of indices to output: all indices `1 … n` **except** `i+1` (convert to 1‑based).  
   d. Print `n‑1`.  
   e. Print the constructed list of indices (order can be natural ascending).  

5. **Finish** – after all test cases are processed, terminate.

6. **Edge‑case sanity checks** (optional, for robustness)  
   * Verify that after removal the remaining sum is indeed composite (even and >2).  
   * Ensure that the array elements are distinct as per input guarantee (no extra handling needed).  

Following this plan yields a solution that runs in well under the limits and satisfies the requirement of returning a maximum‑size subset with a composite sum.

**### Plan Evaluation**

The algorithm correctly handles both cases:

* If the total sum is composite, the whole array (size `n`) is the maximal subset.
* If the total sum is prime (necessarily odd), removing any odd element makes the remaining sum even and larger than 2, hence composite. The subset size becomes `n‑1`, which is maximal under the prime‑sum condition.

The simulation on the provided example succeeds, and the reasoning guarantees correctness for all allowed inputs.

**Result:** **No Need to Modify Plan**.
**Solution Explanation**

For each test case we have distinct positive integers `a1 … an` (`n ≥ 3`).

* Let `S = a1 + … + an`.
* If `S` is **composite** we can take the whole array – it already has the
  maximum possible size `n`.
* Otherwise `S` is **prime**.
  * A prime larger than `2` is odd, therefore `S` is odd.
  * Removing any **odd** element makes the remaining sum even.
    An even number larger than `2` is always composite.
  * Because `S` is odd, at least one odd element exists.
    Removing one odd element gives a subset of size `n‑1`,
    which is the largest possible size when the whole set does not work.

Thus the answer is either all indices, or all except one odd index.

The only auxiliary routine needed is a primality test for numbers up to
`200·100 = 20000`. Simple trial division up to `√x` is sufficient.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
For each test case  

1. read `n` and the array `a`.
2. compute `S = sum(a)`.
3. if `S` is not prime  
    output `n` and the indices `1 … n`.
4. else (`S` is prime)  
    find any index `i` with `a[i]` odd.  
    output `n‑1` and all indices except `i+1`.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs a largest‑size subset whose sum is
composite.

---

**Lemma 1**  
If the total sum `S` is composite, the whole set of indices is a valid
subset of maximum size `n`.

*Proof.* The subset consisting of all elements has sum `S`, which is
composite by assumption. No subset can be larger than `n`. ∎



**Lemma 2**  
If `S` is prime, then `S` is odd and the array contains at least one odd
element.

*Proof.* Any prime larger than `2` is odd; `S ≥ 6`, so `S ≠ 2`.  
If all elements were even, their sum would be even, contradicting that
`S` is odd. Hence an odd element exists. ∎



**Lemma 3**  
Removing a single odd element from a set whose total sum `S` is odd
produces a new sum `S'` that is even and larger than `2`; therefore `S'`
is composite.

*Proof.* `S' = S – odd` → even.  
All numbers are positive, `n ≥ 3`, and the removed element is at most
`200`, so `S' ≥ S – 200 ≥ 6 – 200` (but in any concrete test case
`S'` is at least `4`). The only even prime is `2`; since `S' ≥ 4`,
`S'` cannot be prime, thus it is composite. ∎



**Lemma 4**  
When `S` is prime, any subset of size `n‑1` obtained by deleting one odd
element has a composite sum.

*Proof.* Directly from Lemma&nbsp;3. ∎



**Theorem**  
For every test case the algorithm outputs a subset of maximum possible
size whose sum is composite.

*Proof.* Two cases.

*Case 1:* `S` is composite. By Lemma&nbsp;1 the algorithm outputs the whole
array, which is optimal.

*Case 2:* `S` is prime. By Lemma&nbsp;2 an odd element exists; the algorithm
removes one such element. By Lemma&nbsp;4 the resulting sum is composite.
Any subset larger than `n‑1` would have size `n`, but the whole set sum is
prime, so it is not allowed. Hence size `n‑1` is maximal. ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

For each test case:

* Computing the sum: `O(n)`.
* Primality test of `S` ( ≤ 20000 ): trial division up to `√S` → `O(√S)`,
  at most `O(150)`.
* Scanning for an odd element: `O(n)`.

Overall `O(n + √S)` ≤ `O(100 + 150)` per test case, negligible for the
given limits. Memory usage is `O(n)` for storing the array and indices.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys
import math

def is_prime(x: int) -> bool:
    if x < 2:
        return False
    if x == 2:
        return True
    if x % 2 == 0:
        return False
    r = int(math.isqrt(x))
    for d in range(3, r + 1, 2):
        if x % d == 0:
            return False
    return True

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it))
        a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
        total = sum(a)
        if not is_prime(total):
            out_lines.append(str(n))
            out_lines.append(' '.join(str(i) for i in range(1, n + 1)))
        else:
            # total is prime -> odd, need to drop one odd element
            odd_idx = -1
            for i, val in enumerate(a):
                if val % 2 == 1:
                    odd_idx = i
                    break
            # build list without odd_idx
            indices = [str(i) for i in range(1, n + 1) if i - 1 != odd_idx]
            out_lines.append(str(n - 1))
            out_lines.append(' '.join(indices))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and
conforms to the required input‑output format.

### Problem Understanding  

Petya has a single flower that starts the first day with a height of **1 cm**.  
For each of the next **n** days we are given a binary value `a_i`:

* `a_i = 1` – the flower is watered on day *i*  
* `a_i = 0` – the flower is not watered on day *i*

The growth rules are:

1. **Death rule** – if there are **two consecutive days** without water (`a_i = 0` and `a_{i‑1} = 0`), the flower dies immediately.  
   In this case the answer for the test case is **‑1**.

2. **Growth rule** – on a watered day the flower grows:
   * **+1 cm** if the previous day was **not** watered (`a_{i‑1} = 0` or `i = 1`).  
   * **+5 cm** if the previous day **was** watered (`a_{i‑1} = 1`).  

3. On a non‑watered day the height does **not** change (unless the death rule triggers).

The task: for each test case, simulate the `n` days and output either the final height or `-1` if the flower died.

Constraints are tiny (`t ≤ 100`, `n ≤ 100`), so a straightforward linear simulation is more than enough.

---

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (different from the one above):**  
*“Given a string consisting of characters ‘A’ and ‘B’, determine the length of the longest substring that contains at most one ‘B’.”*

#### Description  
We receive a string `s`. We need the maximum length of a contiguous segment where the number of ‘B’s does not exceed one.  

#### Step‑by‑step Python‑like solution (no actual code)  

1. **Initialize two pointers** `left = 0` and `right = 0` to represent the current window.  
2. **Maintain a counter** `b_cnt` for how many ‘B’s are inside the window.  
3. **Iterate** `right` from `0` to `len(s)-1`:
   * If `s[right]` is ‘B’, increment `b_cnt`.  
   * While `b_cnt > 1`, move `left` forward:
     * If `s[left]` is ‘B’, decrement `b_cnt`.  
     * Increment `left`.  
   * After the inner loop, the window `[left, right]` satisfies the condition; update `max_len` with `right‑left+1` if larger.  
4. After the loop, `max_len` holds the answer.

#### Algorithm discussion  

The algorithm is a classic **sliding window** technique for “at most *k* occurrences of a character”.  
* **Why it works:** The window is always the longest possible ending at the current `right` while respecting the constraint. Expanding `right` adds a new character; if the constraint is broken we shrink from the left until it is satisfied again.  
* **Complexity:** Each index moves at most once → **O(n)** time, **O(1)** extra space.  

#### Planning for the example problem  

1. Read the input string.  
2. Set `left = 0`, `b_cnt = 0`, `max_len = 0`.  
3. Loop `right` over the indices of the string.  
4. Update `b_cnt` when a ‘B’ is encountered.  
5. While `b_cnt > 1`, move `left` forward and adjust `b_cnt`.  
6. Compute current window length and update `max_len`.  
7. After the loop, output `max_len`.  

---

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

**Core idea:** Perform a single left‑to‑right pass over the `n` days, keeping track of:

* `height` – current flower height (starts at 1).  
* `prev_watered` – whether the previous day was watered (`True/False`).  
* Detect the death condition by checking two consecutive zeros.

**Step‑by‑step logic for each day `i` (1‑based):**

1. Read `a_i`.  
2. If `a_i == 0`:
   * If `prev_watered == False` (i.e., yesterday also `0`), the flower dies → answer `-1` for this test case, stop processing further days.  
   * Otherwise set `prev_watered = False` and continue (height unchanged).  
3. If `a_i == 1`:
   * If `prev_watered == True` → add **5** to `height`.  
   * Else → add **1** to `height`.  
   * Set `prev_watered = True`.  

After processing all days without death, output the final `height`.

**Why this works:**  
The rules are local (depend only on the current day and the immediate predecessor). Maintaining a boolean for “was yesterday watered?” is sufficient to decide the growth amount and to detect two consecutive dry days.

**Complexities:**  
* **Time:** O(n) per test case (max 100 operations).  
* **Memory:** O(1) – only a few integer/boolean variables.

**Edge cases to keep in mind**

* `n = 1` – only one day, death cannot happen because we need two consecutive zeros.  
* The first day being watered (`a_1 = 1`) should add **1** (since there is no previous day).  
* The first day being dry (`a_1 = 0`) is fine; death can only occur from day 2 onward.  
* As soon as death is detected, we must ignore the rest of the input for that test case (but still read it to keep input alignment).  

---

### Plan – Detailed step‑by‑step solution for the original problem  

1. **Read number of test cases `t`.**  
2. **Loop over each test case** (`case_idx` from `1` to `t`):  
   a. Read integer `n`.  
   b. Read the list `a` of `n` integers (0/1).  
   c. Initialise  
      * `height = 1` (initial height).  
      * `prev_watered = False` (no day before day 1).  
      * `dead = False`.  
   d. **Iterate through the days** with index `i` ranging `0 … n‑1`:  
      i. `today = a[i]`.  
      ii. **If `today == 0`**:  
          * If `prev_watered == False` → set `dead = True` and break out of the loop (the flower dies).  
          * Else set `prev_watered = False` (no growth).  
      iii. **Else (`today == 1`)**:  
          * If `prev_watered == True` → `height += 5`.  
          * Else → `height += 1`.  
          * Set `prev_watered = True`.  
   e. After the loop, **output**:  
      * `-1` if `dead` is `True`.  
      * otherwise the current value of `height`.  
3. End program.

**Implementation notes (no code):**  

* Use `sys.stdin.read().split()` or `input().split()` to fetch all numbers efficiently.  
* Convert the day values to integers.  
* The break on death must be followed by discarding the remaining days of the current test case (if using a simple `for` loop over the list, breaking is enough because the list is already read).  
* Print each answer on its own line.

Following this plan will correctly simulate the flower’s growth and detect death, satisfying all constraints of the problem.
### Simulation

**Test case 1**

- `n = 3`, `a = [1, 0, 1]`
- Start: `height = 1`, `prev_watered = False`, `dead = False`
- Day 1 (`1`): not previous water → `height = 2`, `prev_watered = True`
- Day 2 (`0`): previous was watered → `prev_watered = False`
- Day 3 (`1`): previous not watered → `height = 3`, `prev_watered = True`
- No death → output `3`

**Test case 2**

- `n = 3`, `a = [0, 1, 1]`
- Start: `height = 1`, `prev_watered = False`, `dead = False`
- Day 1 (`0`): *according to the plan* `prev_watered` is `False`, so the flower would be declared dead immediately.
- The expected answer for this case is `7`, not `-1`.  
  The plan incorrectly treats the “day before day 1” as a non‑watered day, causing a false death detection.

**Test case 3**

- `n = 4`, `a = [1, 0, 0, 1]`
- The plan would also incorrectly die on day 2 (the first `0`) because `prev_watered` is `False` from the start, while the correct behavior is that the flower dies only after **two consecutive** zeros (days 2 and 3).  

**Test case 4**

- `n = 1`, `a = [0]`
- The plan would incorrectly die on the only day, while the correct answer is `1` (the flower survives because there is no second consecutive zero).

The simulation shows that the current plan mis‑detects death when the first day (or any isolated zero) occurs, because it treats the non‑existent day 0 as a “not watered” day.

### Plan Evaluation

**Plan Modification Needed**

The death condition must be based on **two consecutive days with `a_i = 0`**, not on a single zero preceded by an implicit “day 0”.  
A correct implementation should keep a counter of consecutive zeros (or a flag indicating whether the previous day was zero) that is **initially zero** before day 1. The flower dies only when this counter reaches 2. The growth logic remains unchanged.
## New Plan

1. Read integer `t` – number of test cases.  
2. Loop `t` times:  
   a. Read integer `n`.  
   b. Read list `a` of `n` integers (each 0 or 1).  
   c. Initialise  
      * `height = 1` – initial height of the flower.  
      * `prev_watered = False` – indicates whether the previous day was watered.  
      * `prev_zero = False` – indicates whether the previous day was a zero (not watered).  
      * `dead = False`.  
   d. Iterate over the days with index `i` from `0` to `n‑1`:  
      i. Let `today = a[i]`.  
      ii. **If `today == 1`** (the flower is watered):  
          * If `prev_watered` is `True` → `height += 5`.  
          * Else → `height += 1`.  
          * Set `prev_watered = True`.  
          * Set `prev_zero = False`.  
      iii. **Else (`today == 0`)** (the flower is not watered):  
          * If `prev_zero` is `True` → the flower has two consecutive days without water → set `dead = True` and break the loop.  
          * Otherwise → set `prev_zero = True`.  
          * Set `prev_watered = False`.  
   e. After processing the days (or breaking early), output:  
      * `-1` if `dead` is `True`.  
      * Otherwise output the current value of `height`.  
3. End.
```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it))
        a = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(n)]
        height = 1
        dead = False
        prev_watered = False
        consecutive_zero = 0
        for today in a:
            if today == 1:
                if prev_watered:
                    height += 5
                else:
                    height += 1
                prev_watered = True
                consecutive_zero = 0
            else:  # today == 0
                consecutive_zero += 1
                if consecutive_zero == 2:
                    dead = True
                    break
                prev_watered = False
        out_lines.append("-1" if dead else str(height))
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

### Problem Understanding  

We are given an integer `n` ( 1 ≤ n ≤ 10¹⁸ ).  
For each test case we must output two integers `l` and `r` such that  

* ‑10¹⁸ ≤ l < r ≤ 10¹⁸*  

and the sum of the consecutive integer sequence from `l` to `r` (inclusive) equals `n`:

\[
l + (l+1) + \dots + r = n .
\]

It is guaranteed that at least one pair `(l, r)` exists for every `n`.  
If several pairs satisfy the condition we may output any of them.

The core observation is that the sum of an arithmetic progression with difference 1 is

\[
S = \frac{(l+r)\times (r-l+1)}{2}.
\]

We need to find any `l < r` (both within the allowed bounds) that makes `S = n`.

Because the limits are huge (up to 10¹⁸) we must use O(1) or O(log n) arithmetic per test case, not enumeration.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (different but related):**  
Given an integer `n`, find two positive integers `a` and `b` (`a ≤ b`) such that the sum of all integers from `a` to `b` equals `n`. If multiple solutions exist, output any.

**Description**  
The sum of consecutive integers from `a` to `b` is  

\[
\frac{(a+b)(b-a+1)}{2}=n .
\]

The task is to find a factorisation of `2n` into two numbers with the same parity (both odd or both even) because `(a+b)` and `(b-a+1)` must have the same parity to make `a` and `b` integers.

**Step‑by‑step solution outline (no code)**  

1. Compute `m = 2 * n`.  
2. Iterate over possible lengths `len = b-a+1` (starting from 1).  
   * For each `len` that divides `m`, compute `sumEnds = m / len`.  
   * `sumEnds` equals `a + b`.  
   * Check that `sumEnds` and `len` have the same parity (both odd or both even).  
   * If they do, solve the linear system:  
        * `a + b = sumEnds`  
        * `b - a + 1 = len` → `b - a = len - 1`  
        * Adding the two equations gives `2b = sumEnds + len - 1`, so `b = (sumEnds + len - 1)/2`.  
        * Then `a = sumEnds - b`.  
   * Verify `a ≥ 1`. If true, we have a valid pair.  

3. Because `len` grows, the first found pair is sufficient. The loop runs up to `√(2n)` which is fast for `n ≤ 10⁹`.

**Algorithm discussion**  

* The key is factorising `2n`.  
* Parity condition guarantees integer `a` and `b`.  
* Complexity is `O(√n)` per test case, acceptable for moderate limits.  

**Planning for this example**  

1. Read `t`.  
2. For each test case read `n`.  
3. Compute `m = 2*n`.  
4. Loop `len` from 1 to `int(sqrt(m))`.  
5. If `m % len == 0`, compute `sumEnds = m // len`.  
6. Check parity, compute `a` and `b`, output if valid.  

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

The original problem relaxes the sign restriction: `l` and `r` may be negative, zero, or positive, as long as they stay inside `[-10¹⁸, 10¹⁸]`. This extra freedom makes the construction trivial.

**Key insight**  

For any integer `n` we can always represent it as the sum of **two** consecutive integers:

\[
n = (n-1) + n .
\]

But this uses `l = n-1`, `r = n`, and the condition `l < r` holds. However `l` may be as large as `10¹⁸‑1`, which is still within the allowed range because `n ≤ 10¹⁸`. The lower bound `‑10¹⁸` is also satisfied because `n‑1 ≥ 0` for all given `n`. So the pair `(n‑1, n)` is always a valid answer.

Even simpler, we can use the pair `l = - (n-1)` and `r = n`. The sum of the arithmetic progression from `- (n-1)` to `n` is

\[
\frac{(- (n-1) + n) \times (n - (-(n-1)) + 1)}{2}
= \frac{1 \times (2n)}{2}=n .
\]

Here `l = -(n-1)` is always ≥ ‑10¹⁸ because `n ≤ 10¹⁸`.  
`r = n` ≤ 10¹⁸.  
And `l < r` because `-(n-1) < n` for any positive `n`.  

Thus a universal construction exists:

* **Option A (two numbers):** `l = n-1`, `r = n`.  
* **Option B (symmetric around 0):** `l = -(n-1)`, `r = n`.  

Both satisfy the bounds and the sum condition. The problem statement only requires `l < r`, not that the length of the segment be at least 2, but `l < r` guarantees at least two numbers, which is fine.

**Why it always works**  

The sum of an arithmetic progression with first term `a` and last term `b` and step 1 is  

\[
S = \frac{(a+b)(b-a+1)}{2}.
\]

Choosing `a = -(n-1)` and `b = n` gives  

* `a + b = -(n-1) + n = 1` (odd).  
* `b - a + 1 = n - (-(n-1)) + 1 = 2n`.  

Hence  

\[
S = \frac{1 \times 2n}{2}=n .
\]

All constraints are respected because `|a|,|b| ≤ 10^{18}`.

**Complexities**  

* Time: O(1) per test case.  
* Memory: O(1).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Plan – step‑by‑step solution for the original problem  

1. **Read input**  
   * Read integer `t` – number of test cases.  
   * Loop `t` times, reading each `n` (as a Python `int`, which handles up to 10¹⁸ easily).

2. **Construct answer**  
   * For each `n`, compute  
        * `l = -(n-1)`  
        * `r = n`  
   * (Alternatively `l = n-1`, `r = n`; both are correct.)

3. **Output**  
   * Print `l` and `r` separated by a space on a single line for each test case.

4. **Correctness check (optional, for reasoning)**  
   * Verify that `-10^{18} ≤ l < r ≤ 10^{18}` – holds because `0 ≤ n ≤ 10^{18}`.  
   * Verify the sum: using the arithmetic‑progression formula, the sum equals `n`.  

5. **Complexity analysis**  
   * The algorithm performs a constant amount of arithmetic per test case → O(t) total time, O(1) extra memory.

That completes the plan. Implement the described steps in Python 3, respecting the input‑output format.

| 3000000000000 | -2999999999999 | 3000000000000 | `-2999999999999 3000000000000` |

All these pairs satisfy  

- `-10^{18} ≤ l < r ≤ 10^{18}` (because `1 ≤ n ≤ 10^{18}`), and  
- the sum of all integers from `l` to `r` equals `n` (the arithmetic‑progression formula gives `(l+r)*(r-l+1)/2 = n`).

Thus the produced output is a correct answer for each test case (any valid answer is acceptable, not necessarily the one shown in the statement).

### Plan Evaluation

The algorithm correctly constructs a pair `(l, r)` for every possible `n` within the required limits, runs in `O(t)` time and `O(1)` extra memory, and matches the problem constraints.

**No Need to Modify Plan**.
```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    out_lines = []
    for i in range(1, t + 1):
        n = int(data[i])
        l = -(n - 1)
        r = n
        out_lines.append(f"{l} {r}")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

### Problem Understanding  

We have a board that consists of exactly two rows and **n** columns ( 3 ≤ n ≤ 100 ).  
The start cell is the upper‑left corner (1, 1) and the target cell is the lower‑right corner (2, n).  
Each cell is either safe (`0`) or a trap (`1`). The start and target cells are guaranteed to be safe.  

In one move the character may go to any of the at most eight neighbouring cells (horizontal, vertical or diagonal) as long as the move stays inside the board and the destination cell is safe.  

For each test case we have to answer **YES** if there exists any sequence of such moves that brings the character from the start to the target, otherwise **NO**.

The constraints are tiny (only two rows, at most 100 columns, up to 100 test cases), therefore a simple graph‑search (BFS/DFS) or a greedy observation is sufficient.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

## Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (different from the original):**  
*“Given a 2 × n grid where each cell contains a non‑negative integer, you start at (1, 1) and want to reach (2, n). In one step you may move right, down, or diagonally down‑right (i.e. from (r, c) to (r, c+1), (r+1, c), or (r+1, c+1)). The cost of a path is the sum of the visited cells. Find the minimum possible cost.”*

#### Description  
- Two rows, n columns, each cell has a weight.  
- Allowed moves: right, down, diagonal down‑right.  
- Start at top‑left, finish at bottom‑right.  
- Need the minimum total weight of a feasible path.

#### Step‑by‑step Python‑like solution (no actual code)  

1. **Read input** – number of test cases, then for each case read `n` and the two rows of integers.  
2. **Dynamic programming table** `dp[2][n]` where `dp[r][c]` stores the minimal cost to reach cell `(r, c)`.  
3. **Initialisation** – `dp[0][0] = grid[0][0]`. All other cells are set to a large number (`inf`).  
4. **Iterate columns from left to right**:  
   - For each row `r` (0 or 1) and column `c` (0 … n‑1) that is reachable (`dp[r][c] != inf`):  
        *Try the three moves*  
        - Right: update `dp[r][c+1]` with `dp[r][c] + grid[r][c+1]` (if `c+1 < n`).  
        - Down: update `dp[1‑r][c]` with `dp[r][c] + grid[1‑r][c]`.  
        - Diagonal: update `dp[1‑r][c+1]` with `dp[r][c] + grid[1‑r][c+1]` (if `c+1 < n`).  
5. **Result** – after processing all columns, answer is `dp[1][n‑1]`. Print it.  

#### Algorithm discussion  

- The DP works because the allowed moves never go left or up, so the state of a cell depends only on cells that are already processed (same column or previous column).  
- Complexity: O(n) per test case, O(1) extra memory besides the DP table (2 × n).  
- This is a classic “grid DP with monotone moves” problem.

#### Planning to solve the example problem  

1. Parse input.  
2. Build a 2 × n integer matrix.  
3. Create a DP matrix filled with `inf`.  
4. Set the start cell.  
5. Loop over columns, for each reachable cell try the three moves and relax the DP values.  
6. Output the DP value at the target cell.  

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

The original problem is a reachability question on a tiny graph:

*Vertices* – the safe cells of the 2 × n board.  
*Edges* – between any two cells whose row difference ≤ 1 and column difference ≤ 1 (the eight‑neighbourhood).  

Because the board is tiny, we can simply run a **Breadth‑First Search (BFS)** or **Depth‑First Search (DFS)** from the start cell and see whether the target cell is visited.

**Why BFS/DFS works:**  
- The graph has at most `2 × n ≤ 200` vertices, so traversal is trivial.  
- All edges have equal “cost”, we only need existence of a path, not shortest length.  

**Alternative greedy observation (often used in editorial):**  
If we look at columns where both cells are traps (`11`), they act as a wall that cannot be crossed. The only way to pass from left to right is to never step on a column that contains a trap in the row we are currently on *and* the opposite row simultaneously. Hence the answer is **YES** iff there is **no column `i` (2 ≤ i ≤ n‑1) where both cells are `1`**.  
Why? Because with diagonal moves we can always “switch rows” in a column that has a single `1`, but a column with `11` blocks any passage – you cannot step into either cell of that column, so you cannot go from columns `< i` to columns `> i`.  

Both approaches are correct; BFS is straightforward to implement, the column‑wall check is O(n) and even simpler.

#### Tutorial / How to approach this type of algorithm  

1. **Model the problem as a graph** – each safe cell is a node, adjacency defined by the movement rule.  
2. **Choose a traversal method** – BFS for shortest‑path style, DFS for simple reachability.  
3. **Implement visited tracking** – a 2 × n boolean array.  
4. **Queue/stack initialization** – start from (0, 0) (using 0‑based indices).  
5. **Process neighbours** – for each of the eight possible direction vectors `dx ∈ {‑1,0,1}`, `dy ∈ {‑1,0,1}` (skip (0,0)).  
   - Check bounds (`0 ≤ nx < 2`, `0 ≤ ny < n`).  
   - Skip if the neighbour cell is a trap (`'1'`).  
   - Skip if already visited.  
   - Mark visited and push to queue/stack.  
6. **Termination** – when the queue empties or when the target cell (1, n‑1) is visited.  
7. **Output** – “YES” if visited, otherwise “NO”.  

**Important considerations**  
- Remember that diagonal moves are allowed, so there are up to eight neighbours, not just four.  
- The start and target cells are guaranteed safe, but still check them when reading input.  
- Because the board has only two rows, the neighbour generation can be hard‑coded (e.g., for a cell in row 0 you can only go to rows 0 or 1).  
- The wall‑column observation is a quick shortcut: just scan the strings and look for a column where both characters are `'1'`. If such a column exists, answer is “NO”; otherwise “YES”.  

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Plan – Detailed step‑by‑step solution for the original problem  

1. **Read the number of test cases `t`.**  
2. **Loop over each test case:**  
   a. Read `n`.  
   b. Read the two strings `row0` and `row1` (each of length `n`).  
   c. **Fast check (optional but simplest):**  
      - For every column index `i` from `1` to `n‑2` (0‑based), examine `row0[i]` and `row1[i]`.  
      - If both are `'1'`, immediately output `NO` for this test case and continue to the next case.  
   d. If the scan finishes without finding a `11` column, output `YES`.  
   *(If you prefer the explicit graph traversal, replace steps c–d with a BFS as described below.)*  

   **BFS version (alternative):**  
   - Create a 2 × n boolean array `vis` initialized to `False`.  
   - Initialise a queue with the start cell `(0, 0)` and set `vis[0][0] = True`.  
   - While the queue is not empty:  
        * Pop a cell `(r, c)`.  
        * If `(r, c)` is the target `(1, n‑1)`, break and answer `YES`.  
        * For each `dr` in `{-1, 0, 1}` and each `dc` in `{-1, 0, 1}` (skip `dr == 0 and dc == 0`):  
            + Compute `nr = r + dr`, `nc = c + dc`.  
            + If `0 ≤ nr < 2` and `0 ≤ nc < n` and not visited and the board cell is `'0'`, mark visited and push `(nr, nc)` into the queue.  
   - After the loop, if the target was never visited, answer `NO`.  

3. **Print the answer** (`YES` or `NO`) for each test case on its own line.  

The overall complexity is O(t · n) (linear scan) or O(t · n) with BFS (still linear because the graph has ≤ 200 vertices). Memory usage is O(n) per test case. This satisfies the given limits easily.

**Plan Evaluation**

The simulation shows that the simple scan for a column where both cells are traps correctly decides each test case, matching the official answers. Therefore the plan works for the given problem and needs no changes.

**Result:** **No Need to Modify Plan**.
**Solution Explanation**

For each test case we are given two strings `a` and `b` of length `n`
(`a[i]` – cell `(1,i+1)`, `b[i]` – cell `(2,i+1)`).
A cell is blocked if its character is `'1'`.

The character can move like a king in chess: in one step it may change the
row by at most `1` and the column by at most `1`.  
Consequently the column index can increase by **at most one** in a step,
so every column from `1` to `n` must be visited at least once.

If some column `i` contains two blocked cells (`a[i] = b[i] = '1'`) then
the character cannot stand in this column at all, therefore it can never
reach the last column.  
Conversely, if **no** column has both cells blocked, we can always walk
through the grid: start at `(1,1)`, move right while staying in the same row
as long as the next cell is free, and when a blocked cell appears we can
switch to the other row (the other cell of the same column is free by the
assumption). Repeating this we finally reach `(2,n)`.

Thus the level is solvable **iff** there is no column where both cells are
traps.

The algorithm is therefore a single linear scan of the columns.

---

#### Algorithm
For each test case  

1. read `n`, strings `a`, `b`.  
2. for `i = 0 … n‑1`  
   * if `a[i] == '1'` **and** `b[i] == '1'` → answer `NO` for this case.  
3. if the scan finishes without such a column → answer `YES`.

---

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs `YES` exactly for solvable levels.

*Lemma 1*  
If a column contains two blocked cells, the level is unsolvable.

*Proof.*  
The character can increase its column index by at most one per move,
hence it must occupy some cell of every column on its way from column 1 to
column n. In a column where both cells are blocked there is no cell to
stand on, so the character cannot pass this column. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
If no column contains two blocked cells, the level is solvable.

*Proof.*  
Consider the walk that proceeds column by column from left to right.
At column `i` we are in some row `r`.  
If the cell `(r,i+1)` is free we move straight to it.  
Otherwise that cell is blocked, but because the column `i+1` does **not**
contain two blocked cells, the opposite cell `(3‑r,i+1)` is free.
We move diagonally to that free cell, thereby switching rows.
Thus we can always advance to the next column.
Starting from `(1,1)` (free) and repeating this step we reach column `n`.
The target cell `(2,n)` is guaranteed to be free, so we finish there.
∎



*Theorem*  
For every test case the algorithm prints `YES` iff the level can be
completed.

*Proof.*  
- If the algorithm prints `NO`, it has found a column with two blocked
  cells. By Lemma&nbsp;1 the level is unsolvable.
- If the algorithm prints `YES`, no such column exists.
  By Lemma&nbsp;2 a valid path exists, so the level is solvable.

Both directions hold, completing the proof. ∎



---

#### Complexity Analysis  

Scanning the two strings once costs `O(n)` time and `O(1)` extra memory.
With at most `t ≤ 100` test cases the total complexity is `O(t·n)`.

---

#### Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    it = iter(sys.stdin.read().strip().split())
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it))
        a = next(it).strip()
        b = next(it).strip()
        possible = True
        for i in range(n):
            if a[i] == '1' and b[i] == '1':
                possible = False
                break
        out_lines.append("YES" if possible else "NO")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.

### Problem Understanding  

We are given  

* `N` distinct positive integers `A[1…N]` – the masses of the available weights.  
* A string `S` of length `N` consisting of the letters **L** and **R**.  

During the process we may pick the weights in **any order** we like.  
When we pick a weight `w` we must decide on which pan of the balance to put it  
(`L` → left pan, `R` → right pan).  

Let  

```
D = (total mass on left) – (total mass on right)
```

Initially `D = 0`.  
After the *i‑th* move the sign of `D` must agree with `S[i]` :

* `S[i] = 'L'`  →  after the move `D > 0` (left side heavier)  
* `S[i] = 'R'`  →  after the move `D < 0` (right side heavier)

We have to output a concrete sequence of moves (weight value + side) that satisfies all
`N` conditions, or output `-1` if it is impossible.

The constraints are up to `2·10⁵` weights, therefore an `O(N log N)` solution is required.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (different from the current one)**  

> *You are given a multiset of integers. You have to output them in an order such that the
>  prefix sums are strictly increasing.*  

**Algorithm Sketch**  

* Sort the numbers increasingly.  
* Output them in that order – because each new number is at least as large as the previous
>  one, the prefix sum grows strictly.

**Python‑like Pseudocode**

```python
def increasing_prefix(nums):
    nums.sort()
    cur = 0
    for x in nums:
        cur += x
        print(cur)          # cur is strictly larger than the previous prefix sum
```

**Discussion**  

* Sorting gives the smallest possible growth at each step, guaranteeing the condition.
* The algorithm runs in `O(N log N)` because of the sort; the output loop is `O(N)`.

**Planning for the Example Problem**  

1. Read the list.  
2. Sort it.  
3. Iterate, maintain a running sum, and print the sum after each insertion.  

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

The task can be seen as constructing a signed sequence `b₁, b₂, …, b_N` where each
`b_i` is either `+w` (weight `w` placed on the left) or `‑w` (weight `w` placed on the right).
The cumulative sum after the `i`‑th element must have the sign prescribed by `S[i]`.

Let  

```
need_i = +1   if S[i] == 'L'      (we need D > 0 after this move)
need_i = -1   if S[i] == 'R'      (we need D < 0 after this move)
```

Assume the current difference before step `i` is `D`.  
Two possibilities exist:

| Current sign of `D` | Desired sign `need_i` | What we have to do |
|---------------------|----------------------|--------------------|
| `sign(D) == need_i` (already correct) | keep the sign | • Put any remaining weight on the **same** side – sign stays the same.<br>• Or put a *small* weight on the opposite side, provided it does **not** overturn the sign (i.e. its mass `< |D|`). |
| `sign(D) != need_i` (wrong sign or `D = 0`) | flip the sign | We must add a weight **larger** than `|D|` on the side `need_i`. The added mass dominates the previous imbalance and forces the required sign. |

Consequences for a constructive greedy algorithm  

* When the sign is already correct we can safely use the **smallest** remaining weight
  (put it on the side `need_i`).  
* When we have to flip the sign we need a weight **greater** than `|D|`.  
  The **largest** remaining weight is the best candidate – if even the largest one is
  not enough, the task is impossible.

Because we only ever need “smallest” and “largest” remaining weight, a simple
two‑pointer technique on a sorted array is sufficient:

1. Sort all masses increasingly: `W[0] < W[1] < … < W[N‑1]`.  
2. Keep two indices  
   * `l` – points to the smallest unused weight (`l` starts at `0`).  
   * `r` – points to the largest unused weight (`r` starts at `N‑1`).  
3. Process the string `S` from left to right, maintaining the current difference `D`.  

   For step `i` (`need = +1` for `L`, `‑1` for `R`):

   * **If** `D * need > 0` (sign already correct)  
        *Take* `w = W[l]`, `l += 1`.  
        *Place* it on side `need` (`L` if `need = +1`, `R` otherwise).  
        *Update* `D += need * w`.  

   * **Else** (`D * need ≤ 0`) – we must flip the sign.  
        *Take* `w = W[r]`, `r -= 1`.  
        *If* `w ≤ |D|` → even the largest remaining weight cannot overturn the balance → **impossible**.  
        *Otherwise* place it on side `need` and update `D += need * w`.

4. Record each chosen weight together with the side on which it was placed.
5. If we finish all `N` steps, output the recorded moves; otherwise output `-1`.

**Why the greedy works**

*When the sign is already correct* – adding any positive amount on the same side
keeps the sign, and using the smallest weight leaves larger weights for future
steps where they may be needed to flip the sign.

*When the sign is wrong* – the only way to change the sign is to add a weight
larger than the current absolute imbalance. The largest remaining weight is the
only candidate that can possibly satisfy `w > |D|`. If it fails, no other weight
can succeed, therefore the instance is unsolvable.

The algorithm touches each weight exactly once, runs in `O(N log N)` for the
initial sort and `O(N)` afterwards, and uses `O(N)` additional memory for the
output list.

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Plan – step‑by‑step solution for the original problem  

1. **Input reading**  
   * Read integer `N`.  
   * Read the list of `N` distinct integers `A`.  
   * Read the string `S`.  

2. **Pre‑processing**  
   * Sort `A` in non‑decreasing order → array `W`.  
   * Initialise two pointers: `l = 0` (smallest unused), `r = N‑1` (largest unused).  
   * Set current difference `D = 0`.  
   * Prepare an empty list `answer` that will store tuples `(weight, side_char)`.

3. **Main loop (for i = 0 … N‑1)**  
   * Determine `need = +1` if `S[i] == 'L'` else `‑1`.  
   * **Case 1 – sign already correct** (`D * need > 0`):  
        * `w = W[l]`; `l += 1`.  
        * `side = 'L'` if `need == +1` else `'R'`.  
        * Append `(w, side)` to `answer`.  
        * Update `D += need * w`.  
   * **Case 2 – sign not correct** (`D * need ≤ 0`):  
        * `w = W[r]`; `r -= 1`.  
        * If `w <= abs(D)`:  
            * Print `-1` and terminate the program (no solution).  
        * Otherwise:  
            * `side = 'L'` if `need == +1` else `'R'`.  
            * Append `(w, side)` to `answer`.  
            * Update `D += need * w`.  

4. **After the loop**  
   * If we never terminated early, we have a valid sequence.  
   * Output each pair from `answer` on its own line: `weight` followed by a space and the side character.

5. **Complexity analysis**  
   * Sorting: `O(N log N)`.  
   * Loop: `O(N)` (each weight is taken exactly once).  
   * Memory: `O(N)` for the sorted array and the answer list.

6. **Edge cases to keep in mind**  
   * `N = 1` – the algorithm correctly picks the only weight on the required side.  
   * Initial `D = 0` – treated as “sign not correct”, so the first move always uses the
     largest weight, guaranteeing a non‑zero imbalance.  
   * When `|D|` becomes very large, the algorithm will automatically start using the
     smallest remaining weights because the sign will already be correct.  

Following this plan yields a correct, efficient implementation in Python 3.
### Simulation

**Sample input**

```
5
3 8 2 13 7
LLRLL
```

**Applying the given plan**

1. Sort the weights: `W = [2, 3, 7, 8, 13]`  
   `l = 0`, `r = 4`, `D = 0`, `answer = []`.

2. **i = 0**, `S[0] = 'L'` → `need = +1`  
   `D * need = 0` → not > 0, so *Case 2*.  
   Take the largest weight `w = W[r] = 13`, `r = 3`.  
   `13 ≤ |0|` is false → place on **L**.  
   `answer ← (13, L)`, `D = 0 + 13 = 13`.

3. **i = 1**, `S[1] = 'L'` → `need = +1`  
   `D * need = 13 > 0` → *Case 1*.  
   Take the smallest weight `w = W[l] = 2`, `l = 1`.  
   Place on **L**.  
   `answer ← (2, L)`, `D = 13 + 2 = 15`.

4. **i = 2**, `S[2] = 'R'` → `need = -1`  
   `D * need = 15·(-1) = -15 ≤ 0` → *Case 2*.  
   Take the largest remaining weight `w = W[r] = 8`, `r = 2`.  
   Check `w ≤ |D|` → `8 ≤ 15` **is true**.  
   According to the plan we must output `-1` and stop.

The algorithm stops with `-1`, while the sample output shows a valid sequence:

```
3 L
2 R
8 R
13 L
7 L
```

Thus the plan fails on the provided example.

### Plan Evaluation

**Plan Modification Needed** – the current greedy strategy (always using the largest remaining weight when the required sign differs from the current imbalance) can create an imbalance that later cannot be reversed, leading to false “no‑solution” conclusions. A correct algorithm must, when a sign change is needed, select the *smallest* unused weight that is **strictly larger** than the current absolute imbalance, not necessarily the overall largest weight. This requires a data structure that can locate and remove such a weight efficiently (e.g., a balanced binary search tree or a Fenwick tree over the sorted array). The plan must be revised accordingly.
## New Plan

1. **Read input**  
   - Read integer `N`.  
   - Read list `A` of `N` distinct integers.  
   - Read string `S` of length `N` (characters only ‘L’ or ‘R’).

2. **Prepare data structures**  
   - Sort `A` in ascending order and store as list `weights`.  
   - Keep the sorted list as a mutable container that supports “find smallest element > x” and deletion in `O(log N)` time (e.g., a balanced binary‑search tree, or Python’s `bisect` on a list together with `pop`).

3. **Initialize variables**  
   - `diff = 0`
