completed 92/165, Solved: True, number of success = 79/92, acc = 85.87

### Problem Understanding  

We are given a short string `s` (length ≤ 100) that consists only of the letters **a** and **b**.  

* `AB(s)` – how many times the substring **"ab"** appears in `s`.  
* `BA(s)` – how many times the substring **"ba"** appears in `s`.  

In one operation we may pick any position `i` (1‑based) and replace `s[i]` by either **a** or **b** (i.e. we may change the character or keep it).  

The task: modify the string with the **minimum possible number of operations** so that after the modifications  

```
AB(s) == BA(s)
```  

If several optimal strings exist, any of them may be printed.

The constraints are tiny (|s| ≤ 100, up to 1000 test cases), therefore an O(|s|) or even O(|s|²) solution per test case easily fits.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (different from the current one):**  
*Given a binary string `t` (characters ‘0’ and ‘1’), change the minimum number of characters so that the number of occurrences of the substring “01” equals the number of occurrences of “10”.*  

**Description**  
The problem is structurally identical to the original one, only the alphabet is `{0,1}` instead of `{a,b}`. The goal is to balance the two opposite directed edges in the adjacency view of the string.

**Algorithm Sketch (step‑by‑step, no code)**  

1. **Count the two patterns** – scan the string once and count how many times “01” and “10” appear.  
2. **Observe the effect of a single change** – flipping a character at position `i` only influences the two adjacent pairs `(i‑1,i)` and `(i,i+1)`.  
3. **If the counts are already equal** – answer is the original string, zero operations.  
4. **Otherwise** – the difference `d = |cnt01 - cnt10|` is always **odd or even**.  
   * If `d` is **even**, we can fix the imbalance by changing **d/2** characters that lie at the *boundary* between a run of equal characters (e.g. the first character of a “01” or “10” pair).  
   * If `d` is **odd**, we need **(d+1)/2** changes, because each change can modify the difference by at most **2** (turning a “01” into “00” removes one “01” and possibly creates a “10”).  
5. **Construct a concrete solution** – iterate over the string, and whenever we still need to reduce the larger count, flip a character that belongs to a pair contributing to that count (prefer the leftmost such position). Stop when the two counts become equal.  

**Why it works** – each flip reduces the absolute difference by either 1 or 2, and we always choose a flip that reduces it maximally, guaranteeing the minimal number of flips.

**Plan for the example problem**  

*Read the string → count “01” and “10”.*  
*If equal → output original.*  
*Else compute needed flips = ceil(|cnt01‑cnt10| / 2).*  
*Traverse the string, flipping characters that belong to the majority pattern until the required number of flips is performed.*  
*Print the resulting string.*  

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

The original problem is the same as the example, just with letters `a` and `b`.  
Key observations:

1. **Only adjacent pairs matter.**  
   `AB(s)` counts pairs `s[i]='a' , s[i+1]='b'`.  
   `BA(s)` counts pairs `s[i]='b' , s[i+1]='a'`.  

2. **Changing a single character influences at most two pairs** – the pair to its left and the pair to its right (if they exist).  

3. **Effect on the difference**  
   Let `diff = AB - BA`.  
   - If we change a character that participates in an **"ab"** pair, that pair disappears (decreasing `AB` by 1).  
   - If we change a character that participates in a **"ba"** pair, that pair disappears (decreasing `BA` by 1).  
   - At the same time we may create a new opposite pair on the other side, possibly increasing the other count.  
   The net change of `diff` after a single modification is always **-2, -1, 0, +1, or +2**. In practice we can always achieve a change of **±2** by flipping a character that sits at the *border* between two different letters (i.e. the middle of a pattern “ab” or “ba”).  

4. **Minimal number of steps**  
   Because each operation can change `|diff|` by at most **2**, the optimal number of operations is  

   ```
   steps = ceil(|diff| / 2)
   ```

   (If `diff` is already 0, steps = 0.)  

5. **Constructing an optimal string** – we do not need to search; a greedy construction works:  
   * While `AB > BA`, locate a position that belongs to an **"ab"** pair (any `i` such that `s[i]='a'` and `s[i+1]='b'`).  
     * Change either `s[i]` to `'b'` **or** `s[i+1]` to `'a'`.  
     * This removes that “ab” pair and may create a new “ba” pair on the same side, decreasing `diff` by **2** (or by **1** if the other side already matches).  
   * Symmetrically, while `BA > AB`, fix a **"ba"** pair.  

   Because each fix reduces `|diff|` by at least 1 and often by 2, after exactly `ceil(|diff|/2)` fixes the counts become equal.  

6. **Edge Cases**  
   * Length 1 – there are no pairs, counts are already equal.  
   * Strings consisting of a single repeated character – both counts are zero, no changes needed.  

7. **Complexity** – a single linear scan to compute the two counts, then another linear scan (or at most a second pass) to apply the needed flips. Overall **O(n)** per test case, with `n ≤ 100`.  

--------------------------------------------------------------------

### Plan – step‑by‑step solution for the original problem  

1. **Read input** – number of test cases `t`, then each string `s`.  

2. **For each test case**:  

   a. **Count the two patterns**  
      *Initialize `AB = 0`, `BA = 0`.*  
      *Loop `i` from `0` to `len(s)-2`*  
        - If `s[i]=='a'` and `s[i+1]=='b'` → `AB += 1`.  
        - If `s[i]=='b'` and `s[i+1]=='a'` → `BA += 1`.  

   b. **If `AB == BA`** → output `s` unchanged, continue to next case.  

   c. **Determine which count is larger**  
      *If `AB > BA`* → we need to eliminate some “ab” pairs.  
      *Else* → we need to eliminate some “ba” pairs.  

   d. **Compute required number of modifications**  
      `need = (abs(AB - BA) + 1) // 2`   (integer ceiling of half the difference).  

   e. **Greedy fixing loop** – while `need > 0`:  

      *If `AB > BA`* (excess “ab”):  

        - Scan the string from left to right looking for an index `i` where `s[i]=='a'` and `s[i+1]=='b'`.  
        - When such a pair is found, decide which character to flip:  
          *If `i > 0` and `s[i-1]=='b'` then flipping `s[i]` to `'b'` will also create a new “ba” on the left, decreasing `diff` by **2**.  
          *Otherwise flip `s[i+1]` to `'a'` (symmetrical reasoning).  
        - Perform the change on the mutable list representation of `s`.  
        - Update `AB` and `BA` locally (or simply recompute after each change – cheap because length ≤ 100).  
        - Decrease `need` by 1.  

      *If `BA > AB`* (excess “ba”):  

        - Symmetrically look for a “ba” pair (`s[i]=='b' and s[i+1]=='a'`) and flip one of its characters using the same logic.  

   f. **After the loop** the two counts are equal (guaranteed by the math).  

   g. **Convert the mutable list back to a string** and print it.  

3. **End** – all test cases processed.  

**Implementation notes (no code required):**  

* Use a list of characters for easy in‑place modification.  
* Updating `AB` and `BA` after each flip can be done by re‑scanning the whole string (O(n) each time). With `n ≤ 100` and at most `need ≤ 50` flips, the total work per test case stays far below the limits.  
* Alternatively, maintain the counts locally by checking only the affected neighboring pairs, which is also straightforward.  

Following this plan yields a correct string with the minimal number of steps for every test case.
### Simulation

**Test case:**  

```
4
b
aabbbabaa
abbb
abbaab
```

**Step‑by‑step using the original plan**

1. **`b`** – `AB = 0`, `BA = 0`.  
   The plan leaves the string unchanged → output `b`. (Correct)

2. **`aabbbabaa`** – `AB = 2`, `BA = 2`.  
   The plan leaves the string unchanged → output `aabbbabaa`. (Correct)

3. **`abbb`** – `AB = 1`, `BA = 0`, `diff = 1`.  
   The plan computes `need = (|1|+1)//2 = 1`.  
   It finds the pair `ab` at positions 0‑1 and flips the second character (`b → a`).  
   New string becomes `aabb`.  
   Re‑counting: `AB = 1`, `BA = 0` → `diff` is still 1.  
   The required equality is **not** achieved after the single step, so the plan fails for this test.

4. **`abbaab`** – `AB = 2`, `BA = 1`, `diff = 1`.  
   The plan again needs one modification, flips the character inside the first `ab` pair, and ends with a string whose `AB` and `BA` are still unequal.  
   The plan fails here as well.

The original plan does **not** guarantee that each performed modification reduces the difference `|AB‑BA|` by the maximal possible amount (2). Consequently, after the prescribed number of steps the condition `AB = BA` may still be false, as shown in the third test case.

### Plan Evaluation

**Plan Modification Needed**

The plan must be corrected to ensure that after each modification the absolute difference `|AB‑BA|` is reduced as much as possible (by 2 when possible, otherwise by 1). A reliable way is:

1. Compute `diff = AB - BA`.
2. While `diff ≠ 0`  
   * Try flipping every position (`a ↔ b`) and compute the new `diff`.  
   * Choose a flip that makes `|new_diff|` minimal (preferably `|new_diff| = |diff|‑2`).  
   * Apply that flip, update the string and `diff`.
3. The number of performed flips will be exactly `ceil(|initial_diff|/2)`, which is the proven minimum.

With this corrected greedy (or brute‑force) step the algorithm always produces a string with `AB = BA` using the minimal number of changes.
## New Plan

1. **Read input** – integer `t`, then `t` strings `s`.

2. **Process each test case independently**  

   a. Convert `s` to a mutable list of characters `c`.  

   b. Define a helper that for any ordered pair of characters returns its contribution to the difference `AB‑BA`:
      - `"ab"` → `+1`
      - `"ba"` → `‑1`
      - otherwise → `0`

   c. Compute the initial difference  
      `diff = Σ_{i=0}^{n‑2} contribution(c[i], c[i+1])`.  

   d. **Iteratively fix the string while `diff ≠ 0`**  

      i. Initialise `best_i = -1`, `best_diff = diff`.  

      ii. For every position `i` (0 ≤ i < n) evaluate the effect of flipping `c[i]` (changing `a`↔`b`):
          - Let `new_char` be the opposite of `c[i]`.
          - For the left adjacent pair (if `i > 0`):
                * `old_left = contribution(c[i‑1], c[i])`
                * `new_left = contribution(c[i‑1], new_char)`
          - For the right adjacent pair (if `i < n‑1`):
                * `old_right = contribution(c[i], c[i+1])`
                * `new_right = contribution(new_char, c[i+1])`
          - Compute the resulting difference after this flip:  
                `new_diff = diff - old_left - old_right + new_left + new_right`
          - If `|new_diff| < |best_diff|` then set `best_i = i` and `best_diff = new_diff`.  
            (If several positions give the same minimal absolute value, any of them may be kept.)

      iii. Flip the character at `best_i` (`c[best_i] = new_char`).  
           Update `diff = best_diff`.

   e. When the loop ends, `diff` equals 0, i.e. `AB(c) = BA(c)`.

   f. Convert the list `c` back to a string and output it.

3. **Repeat** step 2 for all test cases.
**Solution Explanation**

For a string `s`

* `AB(s)` – number of substrings `"ab"`
* `BA(s)` – number of substrings `"ba"`

For every position `i (0 ≤ i < n‑1)` the pair `s[i]s[i+1]` contributes  

```
+1  if it is "ab"
-1  if it is "ba"
 0  otherwise
```

Let  

```
diff = AB(s) – BA(s) = Σ contribution(s[i],s[i+1])
```

Only the two characters at the ends of the string influence `diff`.

*If we look at the whole string as a walk from the first character to the last,
each time we go from `a` to `b` we add `+1`, each time from `b` to `a` we add `-1`.
All intermediate changes cancel each other, therefore*

```
diff = (last character is 'b') – (first character is 'b')
```

Consequences  

* `diff` can be only `-1, 0, 1`.
* `diff = 0`  ⇔  first and last characters are equal.
* Changing any **inner** character does not modify `diff`.
* Changing the first character (or the last one) flips the sign of its
contribution, i.e. changes `diff` by `±1`.

Hence the minimum number of modifications needed to obtain `AB = BA` is  

```
0  if s[0] == s[-1]
1  otherwise   (change one of the two ends to the other character)
```

Any resulting string with equal ends is acceptable.



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Algorithm
For each test case  

```
if s[0] == s[-1]:
        answer = s
else:
        # change the first character to the last one (or vice‑versa)
        answer = s[-1] + s[1:]
output answer
```

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Correctness Proof  

We prove that the algorithm outputs a string `t` with `AB(t)=BA(t)` and uses the
minimum possible number of changes.

---

**Lemma 1**  
For any string `s`, `AB(s) – BA(s) = (s[-1] == 'b') – (s[0] == 'b')`.

*Proof.*  
Consider the sequence of characters as a walk on the two values `a` and `b`.
Whenever we move from `a` to `b` we add `+1`, from `b` to `a` we add `-1`,
otherwise `0`. The sum of all these changes equals the final value minus the
initial value, i.e. `1` if the string ends with `b` and starts with `a`,
`-1` if it ends with `a` and starts with `b`, and `0` otherwise.
∎



**Lemma 2**  
Changing a character that is **not** the first or the last one does not change
`AB – BA`.

*Proof.*  
Such a character belongs to exactly two adjacent pairs:
`(s[i‑1], s[i])` and `(s[i], s[i+1])`.  
Flipping `s[i]` swaps the contributions of these two pairs, and their sum
remains the same (enumeration of the eight possible triples shows this). ∎



**Lemma 3**  
Flipping the first character (or the last one) changes `AB – BA` by exactly
`±1`.

*Proof.*  
The first character participates only in the pair `(s[0], s[1])`.  
If this pair is `"ab"` its contribution is `+1`; after changing `s[0]` to `b`
the pair becomes `"bb"` with contribution `0`, decreasing the sum by `1`.
All other possibilities change the sum by `0` or `+1`. The same reasoning holds
for the last character. ∎



**Theorem**  
The algorithm outputs a string `t` with `AB(t)=BA(t)` and uses the minimum
possible number of character replacements.

*Proof.*  

*Case 1:* `s[0] == s[-1]`.  
By Lemma&nbsp;1, `AB(s) – BA(s) = 0`; thus `s` already satisfies the requirement.
The algorithm outputs `s` without any change, which is obviously optimal.

*Case 2:* `s[0] != s[-1]`.  
Then by Lemma&nbsp;1, `|AB(s) – BA(s)| = 1`.  
Any modification of an inner character cannot affect the difference
(Lemma&nbsp;2), therefore at least one of the two ends must be changed.
Changing one end flips the sign of its contribution and, by Lemma&nbsp;3,
reduces the absolute difference from `1` to `0`.  
Thus a single change is sufficient and also necessary.  
The algorithm performs exactly one such change, producing a string `t` with
equal first and last characters, hence `AB(t)=BA(t)` (Lemma&nbsp;1). ∎



--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Complexity Analysis  

For each test case we only inspect the first and last characters and possibly
build a new string of length `n`.  
Time complexity: **O(n)** (linear in the string length).  
Memory usage: **O(n)** for the output string (the input itself is given).

--------------------------------------------------------------------

#### Reference Implementation  (Python 3)

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    out_lines = []
    idx = 1
    for _ in range(t):
        s = data[idx]
        idx += 1
        if s[0] == s[-1]:
            out_lines.append(s)
        else:
            # change the first character to match the last one
            new_s = s[-1] + s[1:]
            out_lines.append(new_s)
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input‑output format.

### Problem Understanding  

We are given a **keyboard layout** – a permutation of the 26 lowercase Latin letters.  
The keys are placed in a single row, numbered from left (position 0) to right (position 25).  
For each test case we also receive a word `s` (1 ≤ |s| ≤ 50).  

When typing the word we start with the hand already on the first character – no cost.  
For every subsequent character we must move the hand from the key of the previous character to the key of the current character.  
The time needed for a single move equals the absolute difference of the two key positions.  

The task: for each test case compute the total time (sum of absolute position differences) required to type the whole word.

The problem is straightforward: we only need a mapping from each letter to its index in the given keyboard, then walk through the word and accumulate `abs(pos[current] - pos[previous])`.

---

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (Distinct from the current one):**  
*“Given a string `t` consisting of lowercase letters, find the total distance the finger travels on a standard QWERTY keyboard to type `t`. The keyboard layout is fixed (the usual QWERTY arrangement) and the distance between two keys is the Manhattan distance on the 2‑D grid.”*

**Description**  
- The QWERTY keyboard can be represented as a 2‑D grid (rows of varying length).  
- Each character maps to a coordinate `(row, column)`.  
- Starting from the first character (no cost), the total distance is the sum of Manhattan distances between consecutive characters.

**Algorithm (step‑by‑step, without code)**  

1. **Create a mapping** `char → (row, col)` for all 26 letters based on the QWERTY layout.  
2. **Initialize** `total = 0`.  
3. **Iterate** over the characters of `t` from the second character onward:  
   - Retrieve the coordinates of the current character and the previous character.  
   - Compute the Manhattan distance: `|row_cur - row_prev| + |col_cur - col_prev|`.  
   - Add this distance to `total`.  
4. **Output** `total`.

**Discussion of the algorithm**  
- The mapping is built once, O(1) time (26 entries).  
- The iteration over the word is O(|t|) ≤ 50, trivial.  
- The algorithm uses only integer arithmetic, no special data structures.  

**Planning to solve the example problem**  

1. Write the QWERTY rows as strings, e.g. `["qwertyuiop", "asdfghjkl", "zxcvbnm"]`.  
2. Loop through each row and each character, storing its `(row, col)` in a dictionary.  
3. Read the input word, set `prev = first character`.  
4. For each subsequent character, look up both coordinates, compute Manhattan distance, accumulate.  
5. Print the final sum.

---

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

1. **Read the number of test cases `t`.**  
2. **For each test case:**  
   a. Read the keyboard string `k` (length 26, a permutation of `'a'..'z'`).  
   b. Build a dictionary `pos` mapping each letter to its index in `k` (0‑based).  
   c. Read the word `s`.  
   d. Initialise `time = 0`.  
   e. Set `prev = s[0]`. (No cost for the first character.)  
   f. Iterate over the remaining characters `c` in `s[1:]`:  
      - `time += abs(pos[c] - pos[prev])`  
      - `prev = c`  
   g. After the loop, output `time`.  

**Why this works**  
- The keyboard is a single row, so the distance between two keys is simply the absolute difference of their positions.  
- By pre‑computing the position of each letter we can obtain the distance in O(1) per move.  
- The total work per test case is O(26 + |s|) ≈ O(|s|), well within limits (|s| ≤ 50, t ≤ 1000).

**Important considerations**  

- **Zero‑based vs one‑based indexing:** The absolute difference is the same either way, but be consistent.  
- **Input size:** Even with the maximum `t = 1000` and `|s| = 50`, the total number of operations is ≤ 50 000, trivial.  
- **Edge cases:**  
  * Word length = 1 → answer is 0 (no moves).  
  * Keyboard may be any permutation, not necessarily alphabetical.  
- **Data structures:** A simple Python `dict` (or list of size 26) suffices for the mapping.  

---

### Plan  

1. **Setup I/O**  
   - Use `sys.stdin.read().splitlines()` or an iterator to read all lines efficiently.  
   - Parse the first line as integer `t`.  

2. **Loop over test cases** (`for _ in range(t):`)  
   a. **Read keyboard layout** (`keyboard = next_line`).  
   b. **Create position map**:  
      - Initialise an empty dictionary `pos = {}`.  
      - Enumerate over `keyboard` with `enumerate(keyboard)`, storing `pos[letter] = index`.  
   c. **Read word** (`s = next_line`).  
   d. **Initialize total time**: `total = 0`.  
   e. **Set previous character**: `prev = s[0]`.  
   f. **Iterate through the rest of the word** (`for ch in s[1:]:`)  
      - Compute `dist = abs(pos[ch] - pos[prev])`.  
      - Add to `total`.  
      - Update `prev = ch`.  
   g. **Store/print the result** for this test case.  

3. **Output**  
   - Collect all results in a list of strings and finally `print("\n".join(results))`.  

4. **Testing** (optional but recommended)  
   - Verify with the sample input provided.  
   - Test edge cases: single‑letter word, keyboard reversed, maximum length word.  

5. **Complexity analysis**  
   - Time: O(t · |s|) ≤ 5 × 10⁴ operations.  
   - Memory: O(26) for the position map plus O(t) for storing answers.  

Following this plan will produce a correct and efficient solution for the problem using Python 3.

### Plan Evaluation

The simulation confirms that the algorithm correctly computes the required time for each test case.

**No Need to Modify Plan**.
**Solution Explanation**

For each test case we know the exact order of the 26 keys.  
From this order we build a dictionary `pos` that maps every letter to its
index (0‑based).  
While typing the word `s` we start on its first character – no time is spent.
For every subsequent character we add the distance between the positions of
the current character and the previous one:

```
time += |pos[current] - pos[previous]|
```

The sum of all these distances is the required answer.

The algorithm works in linear time in the length of `s` and uses only a
constant‑size map for the keyboard.

---

**Algorithm**
```
read t
for each test case:
    read keyboard string (length 26)
    build pos[letter] = index for all letters in keyboard
    read word s
    total = 0
    prev = s[0]
    for each character c in s[1:]:
        total += abs(pos[c] - pos[prev])
        prev = c
    output total
```

---

**Correctness Proof**

We prove that the algorithm outputs the exact typing time for each test case.

*Lemma 1*  
`pos[l]` equals the position (0‑based index) of letter `l` on the given keyboard.

*Proof.* By construction we enumerate the keyboard string from left to right,
assigning each encountered letter its enumeration index. Since the keyboard
contains each letter exactly once, the stored index is precisely the letter's
position. ∎

*Lemma 2*  
For any two consecutive characters `a` and `b` in the word `s`, the algorithm
adds `|pos[a] - pos[b]|` to `total`.

*Proof.* The loop iterates over the characters of `s` starting from the second
one, keeping `prev` as the previous character. For each current character `c`
the algorithm computes `abs(pos[c] - pos[prev])` and adds it to `total`. By
definition of `prev`, `c` and `prev` are consecutive characters in `s`. ∎

*Theorem*  
After processing a test case, `total` equals the minimal time required to type
the word `s` on the given keyboard.

*Proof.* Typing starts on the first character without cost. For each subsequent
character the hand must move from the key of the previous character to the key
of the current character. By Lemma&nbsp;1 the positions of these keys are
`pos[prev]` and `pos[curr]`; the movement time is exactly the absolute
difference of these positions. By Lemma&nbsp;2 the algorithm adds this exact
value for every transition between consecutive characters. Summing over all
transitions yields the total typing time, which is therefore stored in
`total`. ∎

Thus the algorithm is correct.

---

**Complexity Analysis**

For each test case:
- Building the position map: `O(26)` time and `O(26)` memory.
- Scanning the word `s` of length `|s| ≤ 50`: `O(|s|)` time.

Overall time: `O(t · |s|) ≤ 5·10⁴` operations.  
Overall extra memory: `O(26)` per test case (constant).

---

**Reference Implementation (Python 3)**

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().splitlines()
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    results = []
    for _ in range(t):
        keyboard = next(it).strip()
        pos = {ch: idx for idx, ch in enumerate(keyboard)}
        s = next(it).strip()
        total = 0
        prev = s[0]
        for ch in s[1:]:
            total += abs(pos[ch] - pos[prev])
            prev = ch
        results.append(str(total))
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(results))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms
to the required input/output format.

### Problem Understanding  

We are asked to construct, for each test case, an increasing sequence  

\[
a_1 < a_2 < \dots < a_n ,
\]

where every element lies in the interval \([1,10^9]\) and, for every adjacent pair, the later element is **not** divisible by the previous one:

\[
a_i \not\equiv 0 \pmod{a_{i-1}}\qquad (2\le i\le n).
\]

The constraints are modest ( \(n\le 1000\) per test, total \( \sum n \le 10^4\) ), and it is guaranteed that a suitable array always exists.  
Thus the task is purely constructive: we must output *any* sequence that satisfies the three conditions.

---

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (different from the current one):**  
*Given an integer \(n\), output a sequence of \(n\) distinct positive integers not exceeding \(10^9\) such that the greatest common divisor of any two consecutive numbers is exactly 1.*

**Description**  
We need a strictly increasing list where consecutive numbers are coprime. A classic way is to use consecutive prime numbers, because any two distinct primes are coprime. Another simple method is to output the first \(n\) odd numbers: \(1,3,5,\dots\). Since any two consecutive odd numbers differ by 2, they are always coprime (their gcd is 1).

**Step‑by‑step Python‑like pseudo‑code**  

1. Read integer \(t\) – number of test cases.  
2. Loop over each test case:  
   a. Read \(n\).  
   b. Initialise an empty list `ans`.  
   c. For `i` from 0 to `n‑1`:  
      - Append `2*i + 1` to `ans` (the i‑th odd number).  
   d. Print the list `ans` separated by spaces.  

**Algorithm discussion**  
- The odd‑number construction guarantees strict increase because each next odd number is larger by 2.  
- Any two consecutive odd numbers are of the form \(2k+1\) and \(2(k+1)+1 = 2k+3\). Their difference is 2, and the only common divisor they could share is 1 (otherwise 2 would divide both, which is impossible because they are odd). Hence \(\gcd = 1\).  
- The largest odd number generated for \(n=1000\) is \(2\cdot 999+1 = 1999\), far below the \(10^9\) bound, so the constraints are satisfied.

**Planning to solve the example problem**  

1. Understand the requirement (strictly increasing, pairwise coprime).  
2. Choose a simple deterministic family of numbers that meets the condition (odd numbers).  
3. Verify the bound \(10^9\) is never exceeded for the maximal \(n\).  
4. Implement the generation loop and output format.  

---

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

**Key observation**  
The only restriction linking two consecutive elements is *non‑divisibility*. If we ensure that each element is **strictly larger** than the previous one **and** that the previous element does **not** divide the current one, the conditions are satisfied.  

A very easy way to guarantee non‑divisibility is to make every element **odd** and to increase by a *fixed odd step* larger than 1, e.g. add 2 each time (the same odd numbers used in the example).  

Why does this work?  

- Let \(a_{i-1}=x\) be odd.  
- Let \(a_i = x+2\) (also odd).  
- If \(x\) divided \(x+2\), then there would exist an integer \(k\) with \(xk = x+2\).  
  Rearranging gives \(x(k-1)=2\). Since \(x\ge 1\) and is odd, the left side is odd (product of odd numbers), while the right side is even – impossible.  
Thus an odd number never divides the next odd number that is exactly two larger.  

Because the sequence is strictly increasing by a constant positive step, the monotonicity condition holds automatically.  

**Bound check**  
Starting from 1 and adding 2 each step, the \(n\)-th element is  

\[
a_n = 1 + 2\,(n-1) = 2n-1 .
\]

For the maximal allowed \(n=1000\), \(a_{1000}=1999\), well below \(10^9\). Hence the construction is always valid.

**Alternative constructions** (useful to know)  

- Use consecutive prime numbers (primes are never multiples of each other).  
- Use numbers of the form \(10^6 + i\) where \(i\) runs from 1 to \(n\); since the difference is 1, divisibility cannot happen unless the previous number is 1, which we can avoid.  
- Use powers of two plus an offset, e.g. \(2^i + 1\); each term is odd and grows quickly, still within the bound for \(n\le 30\).  

The simplest and safest for the given limits is the **odd‑step‑2** construction.

**Algorithm summary**  

For each test case with given \(n\):  

1. Initialise an empty list `ans`.  
2. For `i` from 0 to `n‑1`:  
   - Compute `value = 1 + 2*i` (the i‑th odd number).  
   - Append `value` to `ans`.  
3. Output the list.

All three required properties are satisfied:

- \(1 \le value \le 2n-1 \le 1999 \le 10^9\).  
- Strictly increasing because each step adds 2.  
- No element divides its successor, proved by parity argument above.

---

### Plan – Detailed step‑by‑step solution for the original problem  

1. **Read input**  
   - Read integer `t` (number of test cases).  
   - Loop `t` times, each iteration reading an integer `n`.

2. **Generate the sequence for a single test case**  
   - Create an empty container `seq`.  
   - Iterate `i` from `0` to `n‑1` (inclusive).  
   - For each `i`, compute `odd = 1 + 2*i`.  
   - Append `odd` to `seq`.  
   - After the loop, `seq` holds exactly `n` odd numbers: `[1,3,5,…,2n‑1]`.

3. **Output**  
   - Convert `seq` to a space‑separated string.  
   - Print the string on its own line.  
   - Repeat for every test case.

4. **Correctness proof sketch** (optional, but useful for confidence)  
   - *Range*: For any `i`, `odd = 1 + 2*i ≤ 1 + 2·(n‑1) = 2n‑1 ≤ 1999 < 10^9`.  
   - *Strictly increasing*: `odd_{i+1} - odd_i = 2 > 0`.  
   - *Non‑divisibility*: Suppose `odd_i` divides `odd_{i+1}`. Then `odd_i * k = odd_i + 2` for some integer `k ≥ 2`. Rearranged: `odd_i (k‑1) = 2`. Left side is odd (product of odd numbers), right side is even – contradiction. Hence divisibility never occurs.

5. **Complexity analysis**  
   - For each test case we perform `O(n)` arithmetic operations and store `O(n)` integers.  
   - Overall time: \(O(\sum n) \le 10^4\).  
   - Overall memory: \(O(\max n) \le 1000\). Both well within limits.

6. **Edge cases**  
   - `n = 1`: loop runs once, produces `[1]`, which trivially satisfies all conditions.  
   - Maximum `n = 1000`: produces `[1,3,5,…,1999]`, still inside the allowed range.

7. **Implementation notes** (for the actual code)  
   - Use `sys.stdin.read().split()` for fast input parsing.  
   - Build output lines in a list and join them at the end to avoid many `print` calls.  
   - No need for big‑integer handling; Python’s default `int` suffices.

Following this plan will yield a correct solution for every test case while respecting the problem constraints.

### Plan Evaluation

**No Need to Modify Plan**
**Solution Explanation**

For each test case we have to output any strictly increasing array  
`a1 < a2 < … < an` ( `1 ≤ ai ≤ 10^9` ) such that for every `i ≥ 2`

```
ai is NOT divisible by a(i‑1)
```

The following construction works for every `n`:

```
ai = 2·i + 1          (i = 1 … n)
```

i.e. the odd numbers `3, 5, 7, …`.  

*Why does it satisfy the conditions?*

* **Range** – the largest element is `2·n+1 ≤ 2001` (because `n ≤ 1000`), far below `10^9`.
* **Strictly increasing** – each step adds `2`.
* **Non‑divisibility** – for any `i ≥ 2` we have `a(i‑1) ≥ 3`.  
  If `a(i‑1)` divided `ai`, then `ai = k·a(i‑1)` with integer `k ≥ 2`.  
  The difference `ai – a(i‑1) = 2` would equal `(k‑1)·a(i‑1) ≥ a(i‑1) ≥ 3`, impossible.  
  Hence `ai` is never a multiple of `a(i‑1)`.

Thus the array meets all requirements.

The algorithm is O(n) per test case, easily fast enough.

---

**Algorithm**
```
read t
repeat t times:
    read n
    output the numbers 2*i+1 for i = 1 .. n, separated by spaces
```

---

**Correctness Proof**

We prove that the algorithm outputs a valid array for every test case.

*Lemma 1*  
For any `i (1 ≤ i ≤ n)`, the produced element `ai = 2·i+1` satisfies `1 ≤ ai ≤ 10^9`.

*Proof.*  
`i ≥ 1` ⇒ `ai = 2·i+1 ≥ 3`.  
`i ≤ n ≤ 1000` ⇒ `ai ≤ 2·1000+1 = 2001 < 10^9`. ∎

*Lemma 2*  
The sequence is strictly increasing.

*Proof.*  
`a(i+1) – ai = (2·(i+1)+1) – (2·i+1) = 2 > 0`. ∎

*Lemma 3*  
For every `i ≥ 2`, `ai` is not divisible by `a(i‑1)`.

*Proof.*  
Assume the contrary: `ai = k·a(i‑1)` with integer `k ≥ 2`.  
Then `ai – a(i‑1) = (k‑1)·a(i‑1)`.  
Left side equals `2` (by construction), while right side is at least `a(i‑1) ≥ 3`.  
Contradiction. ∎

*Theorem*  
For each test case the algorithm outputs an array satisfying all three required conditions.

*Proof.*  
By Lemma 1 each element lies in the allowed range.  
Lemma 2 gives strict monotonicity.  
Lemma 3 guarantees the non‑divisibility condition.  
Therefore the produced array is valid. ∎



---

**Complexity Analysis**

For a test case with size `n` we generate `n` numbers and output them.

```
Time   : O(n)
Memory : O(1)   (apart from the output string)
```

The total `n` over all test cases ≤ 10⁴, well within limits.

**Reference Implementation (Python 3)**

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(data[idx]); idx += 1
        # generate odd numbers starting from 3: 2*i+1 for i=1..n
        seq = [str(2 * i + 1) for i in range(1, n + 1)]
        out_lines.append(' '.join(seq))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms to the required input‑output format.

  Therefore the implementation must follow that construction instead of the odd‑number one.
* **Fix** – Replace the generation of odd numbers with a generation of consecutive integers beginning at 2:

  ```python
  seq = [str(i) for i in range(2, n + 2)]
  ```
  This yields exactly the expected output while still respecting all problem limits (`1 ≤ a_i ≤ 10⁹`, strictly increasing, and `a_i` is never divisible by `a_{i‑1}` because consecutive numbers differ by 1).

**Modified Code**

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0])
    idx = 1
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(data[idx])
        idx += 1
        # generate consecutive integers starting from 2: 2,3,...,n+1
        seq = [str(i) for i in range(2, n + 2)]
        out_lines.append(' '.join(seq))
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

1. **Sequence generation** – Switched from odd numbers (`2*i+1`) to consecutive integers beginning at 2 (`range(2, n+2)`).
2. **Correctness** – The new sequence is strictly increasing, each element is at most `n+1 ≤ 1001 < 10⁹`, and for any `i ≥ 2`, `a_i = a_{i-1}+1` cannot be divisible by `a_{i-1}`.
3. **No other modifications** – Input parsing and output formatting remain unchanged. This code now produces the exact output expected by the test cases.

### Problem Understanding

We are given two numbers, each described by a pair \((x, p)\) meaning the integer \(x\) followed by \(p\) zeros, i.e. the value is \(x \times 10^{p}\).  
For each test case we must compare the two numbers and output ‘<’, ‘>’, or ‘=’ accordingly.

The constraints are large: \(x\) up to \(10^{6}\) and \(p\) up to \(10^{6}\). Directly constructing the numbers (by appending zeros) can lead to numbers with up to two million decimal digits, which cannot be stored in normal integer types. We must compare them without building the full numbers.

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (different):** *Compare two fractions a/b and c/d without using floating‑point arithmetic.*  

**Description** – Given four integers \(a,b,c,d\) (positive), determine whether \(\frac{a}{b}\) is less than, equal to, or greater than \(\frac{c}{d}\).  

**Solution Sketch** – Instead of converting to floating point, cross‑multiply: compare \(a \times d\) with \(c \times b\). Use 64‑bit integers or Python’s arbitrary‑precision integers.  

**Algorithm** –  
1. Read \(a,b,c,d\).  
2. Compute `left = a * d`, `right = c * b`.  
3. Compare `left` and `right`.  

**Why it works** – Multiplying both sides of the inequality by the positive denominator \(b d\) preserves the order, and we avoid division and rounding errors.  

**Plan for that problem** – read input, loop over test cases, apply the three‑step comparison, output the appropriate symbol.

---

### Algorithm to solve the original problem

The numbers are \(A = x_1 \times 10^{p_1}\) and \(B = x_2 \times 10^{p_2}\).

**Key observation** – The only difference between the two numbers is the amount of trailing zeros. If we can make the number of trailing zeros equal for both numbers, the remaining “core” part (the original \(x\)) can be compared directly.

Let  

* \(d = \min(p_1, p_2)\).  
* Reduce both numbers by removing \(d\) zeros:  

\[
A' = x_1 \times 10^{p_1-d},\qquad
B' = x_2 \times 10^{p_2-d}
\]

Now at least one of the exponents is zero. The remaining exponent of each number is at most \(10^{6}\) but the other exponent is zero, so the total number of digits of the larger number is at most \( \text{len}(x) + 6\) (since \(x \le 10^{6}\) has at most 7 digits). Therefore we can safely convert the remaining numbers to strings (or keep them as integers) and compare.

If after the reduction the exponents are still different, the one with the larger remaining exponent is definitely larger because we are multiplying the same (or a larger) integer by a power of ten.

**Detailed steps**

1. **Equalise the number of trailing zeros**  
   * Let `k = min(p1, p2)`.  
   * Set `p1 -= k`, `p2 -= k`.  

2. **Now at least one of `p1`, `p2` is zero.**  

   * If `p1 == 0` and `p2 == 0` → both numbers have the same length (no extra zeros). Compare `x1` and `x2` directly.  

   * If exactly one of them is zero, say `p1 > 0` and `p2 == 0`, then we need to compare `x1 * 10^{p1}` with `x2`.  
     * If the number of digits of `x1` plus `p1` is larger than the number of digits of `x2`, then `A > B`.  
     * If it is smaller, then `A < B`.  
     * If the total digit counts are equal, we can pad the shorter `x` with zeros (or multiply the smaller `x` by the appropriate power of ten) until both have the same number of digits, then compare the integer values (they will fit into Python’s big integers because the length is at most about 13‑14 digits).  

3. **Implementation details** –  
   * Use `len(str(x))` to obtain the number of decimal digits of `x`.  
   * When the total digit counts are equal, we can safely compute `x1 * 10^{p1}` and `x2 * 10^{p2}` using Python’s arbitrary‑precision integers (the exponent will be at most about 12‑13, well within memory).  

   * Otherwise, the comparison can be decided solely from the digit counts.

The algorithm runs in \(O(1)\) per test case, well within the limits.

**Important considerations**

* Do **not** try to build the full number when `p` is huge (up to \(10^{6}\)).  
* Use the digit‑count trick to avoid huge numbers.  
* Python’s `int` can hold arbitrarily large values, but constructing a string of length \(10^{6}\) would be memory‑heavy; we avoid it.  
* Edge cases: both numbers equal, both `p` zero, one of the `x` values is 1, etc.

### Plan (step‑by‑step)

1. **Read input** – integer `t`. Loop `t` times.  
2. For each test case:  
   a. Read `x1, p1`.  
   b. Read `x2, p2`.  

3. **Normalise trailing zeros**  
   * `k = min(p1, p2)`  
   * `p1 -= k` , `p2 -= k`  

4. **If both `p1` and `p2` are zero** → compare `x1` and `x2` and output the appropriate symbol.  

5. **Otherwise** (exactly one of `p1`, `p2` is non‑zero)  
   * Let `bigP = max(p1, p2)`, `smallX = x1` if `p1 > 0` else `x2`, `bigX = x2` if `p1 > 0` else `x1`.  
   * Compute `len_big = len(str(bigX)) + bigP` – total digit count of the larger‑exponent number.  
   * Compute `len_small = len(str(smallX))` – digit count of the other number (no extra zeros).  

   * If `len_big > len_small` → the number with the larger exponent is greater → output `'>'` if it is the first number, otherwise `'<'`.  
   * If `len_big < len_small` → opposite.  

   * If the digit counts are equal, we must compare the actual values:  
        * Compute `val_big = bigX * 10^{bigP}` (the exponent is at most about 12‑13, safe).  
        * `val_small = smallX`.  
        * Compare `val_big` and `val_small` and output the result, taking care of the original order (which number is first).  

6. Print the result for the current test case and continue.

The algorithm uses only O(1) extra memory and runs in O(t) time. It respects the limits (t ≤ 10⁴, values up to 10⁶).  

That completes the plan for solving the problem.
### Simulation

We apply the plan to the sample input.

**Test case 1**  
\(x_1=2, p_1=1\) → number = 20  
\(x_2=19, p_2=0\) → number = 19  

- \(k = \min(1,0)=0\); \(p_1=1, p_2=0\).  
- Only the first number has remaining zeros.  
- `len_big = len("2")+1 = 2`, `len_small = len("19") = 2` → equal length.  
- Compare actual values: \(2·10^1 = 20 > 19\).  
- The larger number is the first one → output **'>'**.

**Test case 2**  
\(x_1=10, p_1=2\) → 1000  
\(x_2=100, p_2=1\) → 1000  

- \(k = 1\); after subtraction \(p_1=1, p_2=0\).  
- Only the first number still has zeros.  
- `len_big = len("10")+1 = 3`, `len_small = len("100") = 3` → equal length.  
- Compare: \(10·10^1 = 100 = 100\).  
- Numbers are equal → output **'='**.

**Test case 3**  
\(x_1=1999, p_1=0\) → 1999  
\(x_2=2, p_2=3\) → 2000  

- \(k = 0\); \(p_1=0, p_2=3\).  
- Only the second number has zeros.  
- `len_big = len("2")+3 = 4`, `len_small = len("1999") = 4` → equal length.  
- Compare: \(2·10^3 = 2000 > 1999\).  
- The larger number is the second one → first is smaller → output **'<'**.

**Test case 4**  
\(x_1=1, p_1=0\) and \(x_2=1, p_2=0\).  

- Both \(p\) are zero → compare \(x_1\) and \(x_2\).  
- They are equal → output **'='**.

**Test case 5**  
\(x_1=99, p_1=0\) → 99  
\(x_2=1, p_2=2\) → 100  

- \(k = 0\); \(p_1=0, p_2=2\).  
- Only the second number has zeros.  
- `len_big = len("1")+2 = 3`, `len_small = len("99") = 2`.  
- `len_big > len_small` → the second number is larger → first is smaller → output **'<'**.

The outputs obtained are:

```

=
<
=
<
```

which exactly matches the given example output.

### Plan Evaluation

The step‑by‑step reasoning works for the sample and correctly handles all cases, including the situation where the remaining exponent after removing common zeros can be large (the equality of digit counts can only happen when that remaining exponent is at most the difference of the lengths of the two base numbers, i.e., ≤ 7). Therefore the algorithm is sound and efficient.

**No Need to Modify Plan**.
```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().split()
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    res = []
    for _ in range(t):
        x1 = int(next(it))
        p1 = int(next(it))
        x2 = int(next(it))
        p2 = int(next(it))

        d1 = len(str(x1))
        d2 = len(str(x2))

        total1 = d1 + p1
        total2 = d2 + p2

        if total1 > total2:
            res.append('>')
        elif total1 < total2:
            res.append('<')
        else:
            k = min(p1, p2)
            p1 -= k
            p2 -= k

            if p1 == 0 and p2 == 0:
                if x1 > x2:
                    res.append('>')
                elif x1 < x2:
                    res.append('<')
                else:
                    res.append('=')
            else:
                if p1 > 0:
                    val1 = x1 * (10 ** p1)
                    val2 = x2
                else:
                    val1 = x1
                    val2 = x2 * (10 ** p2)

                if val1 > val2:
                    res.append('>')
                elif val1 < val2:
                    res.append('<')
                else:
                    res.append('=')

    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(res))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

### Problem Understanding  

We have `n` towers (or buildings) with heights `a₁ … aₙ`.  
In one operation we may pick two different indices `i` and `j` and move a single block from tower `i` to tower `j` – i.e. `a_i` decreases by 1 and `a_j` increases by 1.  
We may repeat this operation any number of times (including zero).  

The **ugliness** of a configuration is  

```
ugliness = max(a) – min(a)
```

Our goal for each test case is to obtain the smallest possible ugliness after an arbitrary sequence of moves.

Key observations  

* The total number of blocks `S = Σ a_i` never changes.  
* The operation only redistributes the existing blocks; therefore any final multiset of heights must consist of non‑negative integers that sum to `S`.  
* Because we can move blocks one by one between any pair of towers, we can realise **any** distribution of the `S` blocks across the `n` positions (subject to the integer‑valued heights). In other words, the only restriction is the total sum.  

Consequently the problem reduces to:  

> Given `S` blocks and `n` positions, how can we assign integer heights to the positions so that the difference between the largest and smallest height is as small as possible?

If `S` is divisible by `n`, we can give every tower exactly `S / n` blocks → ugliness = 0.  
If not, the heights must be either `⌊S / n⌋` or `⌈S / n⌉`. The smallest possible spread is therefore `1`.  

Thus the answer for each test case is simply  

```
0, if S % n == 0
1, otherwise
```

---

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (different from the current one):**  
*“Given an array of integers, you may repeatedly choose two indices `i` and `j` (i ≠ j) and transfer 1 unit from `a_i` to `a_j`. Determine the minimum possible **maximum** element after any number of operations.”*  

**Description**  
The operation is the same as in our original problem, but the objective is to minimise the largest value rather than the spread.  

**Step‑by‑step Python‑style solution (no actual code)**  

1. Compute the total sum `S` of the array.  
2. The smallest possible maximum after redistribution is the ceiling of the average, i.e. `ceil(S / n)`.  
   *Proof sketch*: If every element were ≤ `M`, the total sum would be ≤ `n·M`. To accommodate the real sum `S`, we need `M ≥ ceil(S / n)`. Conversely, we can achieve this bound by giving `⌈S / n⌉` to as many positions as needed and `⌊S / n⌋` to the rest.  
3. Output `ceil(S / n)`.

**Algorithm discussion**  

* The algorithm runs in O(n) time per test case (single pass to compute the sum).  
* No need for sorting or complex data structures because the operation allows arbitrary redistribution.  
* Edge cases: when `S` is exactly divisible by `n`, the answer equals `S / n`.  

**Planning to solve the example problem**  

1. Read number of test cases `t`.  
2. Loop over each test case:  
   a. Read `n`.  
   b. Read the list of `n` integers.  
   c. Compute `S = sum(list)`.  
   d. Compute `ans = (S + n - 1) // n` (integer ceiling).  
   e. Print `ans`.  

---

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

**Core idea** – The operation lets us realise any integer distribution of the total number of blocks. Therefore the minimal possible ugliness depends only on whether the average height is an integer.

**Steps**  

1. For each test case, read `n` and the array `a`.  
2. Compute the total sum `S = Σ a_i`.  
3. Check the remainder `r = S % n`.  
   * If `r == 0` → we can give every tower exactly `S / n` blocks → answer `0`.  
   * Otherwise → some towers must receive `⌈S / n⌉` blocks and the rest `⌊S / n⌋` blocks, giving a spread of exactly `1` → answer `1`.  

**Why this works**  

* The sum is invariant, and the operation is fully flexible, so the only limitation is the integer nature of heights.  
* The smallest possible range of integer numbers that sum to `S` over `n` slots is either a single value (when `S` is a multiple of `n`) or two consecutive values (otherwise). No larger spread can be avoided because any distribution must contain at least one value ≤ ⌊S/n⌋ and at least one value ≥ ⌈S/n⌉.  

**Complexity**  

* Time: O(n) per test case (just summing).  
* Memory: O(1) extra besides the input array.

**Important considerations**  

* All heights are positive, but after redistribution some towers could become zero only if `S < n`. However the constraints guarantee `a_i ≥ 1` and `n ≥ 2`, but the algorithm does not rely on positivity; it works for any non‑negative integers.  
* Use 64‑bit integer arithmetic (Python’s `int` is unbounded, so no issue).  

---

### Plan (step‑by‑step)  

1. **Input handling**  
   * Read integer `t`.  
   * Loop `t` times:  
     - Read integer `n`.  
     - Read the line with `n` integers and store them (or process on the fly).  

2. **Compute total sum**  
   * Initialise `total = 0`.  
   * Iterate over the `n` numbers, adding each to `total`.  

3. **Determine answer**  
   * If `total % n == 0` → set `ans = 0`.  
   * Else → set `ans = 1`.  

4. **Output**  
   * Print `ans` (one per line).  

5. **Testing** (offline, not part of final code)  
   * Verify with the sample:  
     - `[10,10,10]` → sum = 30, 30 % 3 = 0 → 0.  
     - `[3,2,1,2]` → sum = 8, 8 % 4 = 0 → 0.  
     - `[1,2,3,1,5]` → sum = 12, 12 % 5 = 2 → 1.  
   * Edge cases:  
     - Minimum `n = 2`, e.g., `[1,1]` → 0.  
     - Large numbers, e.g., `n = 100`, each `a_i = 10⁷` → sum fits in Python int, remainder decides answer.  

6. **Complexity check**  
   * Ensure the loop over each test case runs in O(n) time, total O( Σ n ) ≤ 10⁵ (since `t ≤ 1000` and `n ≤ 100`). Well within limits.  

7. **Finalize**  
   * Write the solution in a clean `solve()` function, call it under `if __name__ == "__main__":`.  

Following this plan will produce a correct, efficient implementation for the problem without any unnecessary complications.

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it))
        total = 0
        for _ in range(n):
            total += int(next(it))
        out_lines.append('0' if total % n == 0 else '1')
    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

### Problem Understanding  

We are given two strings for each test case  

* **S** – an arbitrary lowercase string (|S| ≤ 100).  
* **T** – a permutation of the three‑letter word **“abc”** (so T is one of `abc`, `acb`, `bac`, `bca`, `cab`, `cba`).  

We may reorder the characters of **S** arbitrarily; the result must be a **permutation** of S (i.e. it contains exactly the same multiset of letters).  
Among all such permutations we have to output the **lexicographically smallest** one **that does NOT contain T as a subsequence**.

Key observations  

1. If T is **not** `"abc"` (i.e. its first character is not `'a'` or the order of `'a','b','c'` is not the natural increasing order), then the ordinary sorted string of S (all characters in non‑decreasing order) already avoids T.  
2. The only “dangerous” case is when T = `"abc"` **and** S contains at least one `'a'`, one `'b'`, and one `'c'`. In that situation the sorted string would be `aaa…bbb…ccc…` which certainly has `"abc"` as a subsequence.  
3. For the dangerous case we can keep the overall order lexicographically minimal by:  

   * placing all `'a'` characters first (as usual),  
   * then placing **all `'c'` characters**,  
   * then all `'b'` characters,  
   * and finally the rest of the letters (`'d'` … `'z'`) in normal sorted order.  

   This ordering (`a…c…b…`) breaks the `"abc"` pattern while staying as small as possible because any other rearrangement that moves a character earlier than `'a'` would be larger, and moving a `'b'` before a `'c'` would re‑introduce the subsequence.

Thus the solution reduces to a simple counting / sorting task with a special handling for the exact pattern `"abc"`.

---

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (Distinct from the current one):**  
*Given a string `U` consisting of lowercase letters, output the lexicographically smallest permutation of `U` that does **not** contain the substring `"ab"`.*  

**Description**  
We must reorder the characters of `U`. The ordinary sorted order (`aaaa…bbb…`) would contain `"ab"` as a substring whenever there is at least one `'a'` followed by at least one `'b'`. To avoid the substring we can place all `'b'` characters **before** all `'a'` characters, while keeping the rest of the letters sorted.

**Algorithm Sketch (step‑by‑step, no code)**  

1. Count occurrences of each letter in `U`.  
2. If either `'a'` or `'b'` is missing, the normal sorted string already avoids `"ab"` – output it.  
3. Otherwise, construct the answer:  
   * output all letters `'c'` … `'z'` in sorted order (they are unaffected),  
   * then output all `'b'` characters,  
   * finally output all `'a'` characters.  
   This ordering guarantees that no `'a'` appears before a `'b'`, thus `"ab"` cannot be a substring, and the string is lexicographically minimal under that restriction.

**Discussion of the algorithm**  

* The problem is a variant of “lexicographically smallest permutation with a forbidden pattern”.  
* The key is to identify whether the forbidden pattern can appear in the fully sorted string.  
* If it can, we only need to swap the relative order of the letters that participate in the pattern; all other letters stay sorted.  
* Counting frequencies makes the construction O(|U| + alphabet) which is trivial for the given constraints.

**Plan for the example problem**  

1. Read the input string.  
2. Build a frequency array of size 26.  
3. Check if both `'a'` and `'b'` have positive counts.  
4. If not, output the characters from `'a'` to `'z'` according to their counts (standard sort).  
5. If yes, output:  
   * characters `'c'` … `'z'` in order, each repeated its count,  
   * then `'b'` repeated its count,  
   * then `'a'` repeated its count.  
6. Print the result.  

The same reasoning pattern will be reused for the original problem.

---

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

1. **Count characters** – For each test case, compute the frequency of every letter `'a'` … `'z'` in `S`.  

2. **Detect the special situation** –  
   * If `T` is **not** exactly `"abc"` → the normal sorted permutation (all letters in increasing order) automatically avoids `T`.  
   * If `T` is `"abc"` **and** at least one `'a'`, one `'b'`, and one `'c'` are present in `S` (i.e. their counts are all > 0) → we must modify the order of `'b'` and `'c'`.  

3. **Build the answer**  

   * **General case (T ≠ "abc" or missing a/b/c)**:  
     - Append each letter from `'a'` to `'z'` to the answer, repeating it according to its frequency.  

   * **Special case (T = "abc" and counts of a,b,c > 0)**:  
     - Append all `'a'` characters first (as in the sorted order).  
     - Append all `'c'` characters **next**.  
     - Append all `'b'` characters **after** the `'c'` block.  
     - Append the remaining letters `'d'` … `'z'` in normal order.  

   This ordering (`a…c…b…d…`) is the smallest lexicographic permutation that breaks the subsequence `"abc"` because any earlier change would either increase the string (by moving a larger letter before `'a'`) or re‑introduce the forbidden subsequence.

4. **Output** – Print the constructed string for each test case.

**Why the algorithm works**  

* The lexicographically smallest permutation of a multiset is the sorted order.  
* The only way the sorted order can contain `T` as a subsequence is when `T = "abc"` and the three letters appear in the natural order.  
* Swapping the relative order of `'b'` and `'c'` (placing all `'c'` before `'b'`) eliminates the subsequence while keeping the prefix as small as possible (`a…` cannot be changed).  
* All other letters (`'d'` … `'z'`) are independent of the pattern and stay sorted to preserve minimality.

**Complexities**  

* Counting: O(|S|) per test case.  
* Building the answer: O(26) = O(1) per test case (since alphabet size is constant).  
* Overall: O(total length of input) time, O(1) extra memory besides the frequency array.

---

### Plan (step‑by‑step) for the original problem  

1. **Read the number of test cases `t`.**  

2. **Loop over each test case**  
   a. Read string `S`.  
   b. Read string `T`.  

3. **Count frequencies**  
   - Initialise an array `cnt[26]` with zeros.  
   - For each character `ch` in `S`, increment `cnt[ord(ch) - ord('a')]`.  

4. **Determine whether special handling is needed**  
   - If `T` equals `"abc"` **and** `cnt[0] > 0` (for `'a'`) **and** `cnt[1] > 0` (for `'b'`) **and** `cnt[2] > 0` (for `'c'`), set a flag `special = True`.  
   - Otherwise, `special = False`.  

5. **Construct the answer string**  
   - Initialise an empty list `parts` (or string builder).  

   - **If `special` is False** (normal case):  
     * For `i` from 0 to 25:  
       – Append the character `chr(ord('a') + i)` repeated `cnt[i]` times to `parts`.  

   - **If `special` is True** (T = "abc" and a,b,c all present):  
     * Append `'a'` repeated `cnt[0]` times.  
     * Append `'c'` repeated `cnt[2]` times.   *(note: we skip `'b'` for now)*  
     * Append `'b'` repeated `cnt[1]` times.  
     * For `i` from 3 to 25 (letters `'d'` … `'z'`):  
       – Append `chr(ord('a') + i)` repeated `cnt[i]` times.  

6. **Combine parts** – join the list into a single string `answer`.  

7. **Print `answer`.**  

8. **Proceed to the next test case** until all are processed.

The plan respects the requirement of not providing actual code, while giving a clear, implementable roadmap for solving the problem in Python 3.

**Result:** **No Need to Modify Plan**.
**Solution Explanation**

For each test case we have to output the lexicographically smallest permutation of `S`
that does **not** contain `T` as a subsequence.
`T` is always a permutation of `"abc"`.

*If `T` is not `"abc"`*  
the ordinary sorted string of `S` is already the smallest possible,
because any permutation that is lexicographically smaller would have to start with a
letter earlier than the sorted order, which is impossible.
The sorted string also cannot contain `T` as a subsequence – the only dangerous case
is when `T = "abc"`.

*If `T = "abc"`*  
the sorted string would be  

```
aaaa… bbb… ccc… ddd… …
```

and it obviously contains `"abc"` as a subsequence **iff** the string contains at
least one `'a'`, one `'b'` and one `'c'`.  
To break the subsequence while staying as small as possible we keep all `'a'`
at the front (they are the smallest letters) and then place all `'c'` **before**
the `'b'`.  
All other letters (`'d'` … `'z'`) stay in normal order.

If any of `'a'`, `'b'`, `'c'` is missing, the sorted string cannot contain `"abc"`,
so we output the normal sorted order.

Thus the algorithm is:

```
count letters of S
if T == "abc" and cnt['a']>0 and cnt['b']>0 and cnt['c']>0:
        output:  a…a  c…c  b…b  d…z (each repeated its count)
else:
        output:  a…z in normal order
```

The algorithm is O(|S|) per test case.

---

**Correctness Proof**

We prove that the algorithm outputs the required string for every test case.

*Lemma 1*  
If `T` is not `"abc"` then the sorted permutation of `S` does not contain `T` as a
subsequence.

*Proof.*  
`T` is a permutation of `"abc"` different from `"abc"`, therefore in `T` the
relative order of the three letters is not `a` before `b` before `c`.  
In the sorted string all `'a'` appear before any `'b'`, and all `'b'` appear before
any `'c'`. Consequently the only possible order of the three letters as a
subsequence is `a` → `b` → `c`. Since `T` has a different order, it cannot be a
subsequence. ∎



*Lemma 2*  
Assume `T = "abc"` and `cnt['a'],cnt['b'],cnt['c'] > 0`.  
The string `S* = a…a c…c b…b d…z` (as constructed by the algorithm) does **not**
contain `"abc"` as a subsequence.

*Proof.*  
All `'c'` are placed **before** any `'b'`. Any occurrence of `'a'` is before both
groups. Therefore after an `'a'` we can never see a `'b'` that is followed later
by a `'c'`, because every `'c'` is already before the first `'b'`. Hence the
pattern `a` → `b` → `c` cannot appear. ∎



*Lemma 3*  
Under the same conditions (`T = "abc"` and all three letters present) any
lexicographically smaller permutation than `S*` must contain `"abc"` as a
subsequence.

*Proof.*  
All permutations that are lexicographically smaller than `S*` must coincide with
`S*` up to some position and then have a smaller character at the first differing
position.

The prefix of `S*` consists of all `'a'`. Replacing any `'a'` by a smaller
character is impossible (no smaller character exists).  
Thus the first possible improvement is to place a character **smaller than `'c'`**
immediately after the block of `'a'`. The only such character is `'b'`.  
Putting a `'b'` before the `'c'` block yields the order `a…a b…b c…c …`,
which is exactly the sorted string and it contains `"abc"` (since `'a'`,
then `'b'`, then `'c'` appear in that order). Any other deviation that moves a
character earlier also creates the `a` → `b` → `c` order, because the relative
order of the three groups would become `a` before `b` before `c`. Hence any
lexicographically smaller permutation necessarily contains `"abc"`. ∎



*Lemma 4*  
If `T = "abc"` but at least one of `'a','b','c'` is missing from `S`, the sorted
string does not contain `"abc"`.

*Proof.*  
A subsequence `"abc"` requires each of the three letters to appear at least once.
If any is absent, the subsequence cannot exist. ∎



*Theorem*  
For every test case the algorithm outputs the lexicographically smallest
permutation of `S` that does **not** contain `T` as a subsequence.

*Proof.*  
Consider two cases.

1. `T ≠ "abc"` or at least one of `'a','b','c'` is missing.  
   By Lemma&nbsp;1 (or Lemma&nbsp;4) the sorted string does not contain `T`.  
   Any permutation lexicographically smaller than the sorted order would have to
   place a smaller character earlier, which is impossible. Hence the sorted
   string is the minimal feasible string, and the algorithm outputs it.

2. `T = "abc"` and all three letters are present.  
   The algorithm outputs `S*`. By Lemma&nbsp;2, `S*` avoids the subsequence.
   By Lemma&nbsp;3, no lexicographically smaller permutation can avoid it.
   Therefore `S*` is the required minimal string.

Both cases cover all possibilities, establishing the theorem. ∎



---

**Complexity Analysis**

For each test case we count characters (`O(|S|)`) and then build the answer by
iterating over the 26 letters (`O(26)`).  
Thus time complexity is `O(|S|)` and memory usage is `O(1)` besides the input
string.

---

**Reference Implementation (Python 3)**

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        s = next(it)
        T = next(it)
        cnt = [0] * 26
        for ch in s:
            cnt[ord(ch) - 97] += 1

        special = (T == "abc" and cnt[0] > 0 and cnt[1] > 0 and cnt[2] > 0)

        res_parts = []
        if special:
            # a's
            res_parts.append('a' * cnt[0])
            # c's
            res_parts.append('c' * cnt[2])
            # b's
            res_parts.append('b' * cnt[1])
            # rest d..z
            for i in range(3, 26):
                if cnt[i]:
                    res_parts.append(chr(97 + i) * cnt[i])
        else:
            for i in range(26):
                if cnt[i]:
                    res_parts.append(chr(97 + i) * cnt[i])

        out_lines.append(''.join(res_parts))

    sys.stdout.write('\n'.join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms to
the required input‑output format.

completed 131/165, Solved: True, number of success = 118/131, acc = 90.08
completed 132/165, Solved: True, number of success = 119/132, acc = 90.15

### Problem Understanding  

Polycarp originally had an array **a** of three positive integers \((a_1,a_2,a_3)\).  
From this array he wrote down the sums of **all non‑empty subsequences** (there are \(2^3-1 = 7\) of them), sorted those sums in non‑decreasing order and obtained the array **b** of length 7.  

We are given **b** (guaranteed to be produced by at least one such **a**) and must reconstruct **any** possible triple \((a_1,a_2,a_3)\).  
The constraints are modest: up to 5000 test cases, each value up to \(10^9\).  

Key observations  

* The smallest element of **b** must be the smallest single element of **a**, because any sum that contains more than one element is at least as large as each of its components.  
* The largest element of **b** is the sum of all three numbers: \(a_1+a_2+a_3\).  
* The second smallest element of **b** is the second smallest single element of **a** (or possibly the sum of the two smallest if they are equal to a single element, but the ordering of the three singletons among the seven sorted values is always the first three positions).  
* Consequently, the first three values of **b** are exactly the three individual numbers of **a**, possibly in a different order, because any sum of two or three numbers is ≥ each of the numbers involved.  

Thus we can simply take  

* \(x = b_1\) – the smallest single element,  
* \(y = b_2\) – the second smallest single element,  
* \(z = b_7 - x - y\) – the remaining element, because the total sum \(b_7 = a_1+a_2+a_3\).  

The triple \((x, y, z)\) (in any order) is a valid answer.

---

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (Distinct Example):**  
Given an array **c** of length 5 that contains the sums of all non‑empty subsets of an unknown array **d** of length 2 (so there are \(2^2-1 = 3\) sums, but the array **c** is padded with extra numbers). Recover any possible **d**.

**Description**  
For a 2‑element array \((d_1,d_2)\) the non‑empty subset sums are \(\{d_1, d_2, d_1+d_2\}\). If we are given a sorted list that contains these three numbers (perhaps with duplicates or extra values), we can still retrieve the original numbers by locating the smallest two distinct values and using the largest value as the total sum.

**Algorithm Sketch (step‑by‑step, no code)**  

1. Sort the given list (if not already sorted).  
2. The smallest element is \(d_{\min}=c_1\).  
3. The largest element is the total sum \(S = c_{\text{last}}\).  
4. The second smallest distinct element is the other single element \(d_{\text{other}} = c_2\) (or the next distinct value).  
5. Compute the remaining element as \(d_{\text{remaining}} = S - d_{\min} - d_{\text{other}}\).  
6. Output \((d_{\min}, d_{\text{other}}, d_{\text{remaining}})\) – for length 2 we would just output the two numbers.

**Discussion of the algorithm**  

* The crucial property is that the smallest subset sum must be a single element, because any sum containing more than one element is larger.  
* The largest subset sum is always the sum of all elements.  
* With those two extremes known, the middle elements can be derived by subtraction.  
* This approach works regardless of duplicates, because the ordering guarantees that the first three positions (for three‑element original array) are the individual elements.

**Plan for the example problem**  

1. Read the input list.  
2. Ensure it is sorted (or rely on the problem statement that it already is).  
3. Take the first element as the smallest original number.  
4. Take the last element as the total sum.  
5. Take the second element as the second smallest original number.  
6. Compute the third original number by subtracting the two known numbers from the total sum.  
7. Print the three numbers (any order is acceptable).

---

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

1. **Read the 7 sorted numbers** \(b_1 \le b_2 \le \dots \le b_7\).  
2. **Identify the smallest two single elements**:  
   * \(a_1 = b_1\) (the smallest element of **a**).  
   * \(a_2 = b_2\) (the second smallest element of **a**).  
3. **Compute the total sum** of the three original numbers:  
   * \(S = b_7\) (the largest value in **b**).  
4. **Derive the third element** by subtraction:  
   * \(a_3 = S - a_1 - a_2\).  
5. **Output** the three numbers \((a_1, a_2, a_3)\) in any order.  

**Why this works**  

* In any multiset of three positive integers, the three smallest subset sums are exactly the three individual numbers (they cannot be formed by adding two positive numbers because that would be at least as large as the larger of the two addends).  
* The largest subset sum is always the sum of all three numbers.  
* Knowing two of the three individual numbers and the total sum uniquely determines the third.  

**Edge Cases & Guarantees**  

* The input guarantees that a valid original array exists, so the computed \(a_3\) will be a positive integer.  
* Duplicates are handled naturally: if two original numbers are equal, the first two entries of **b** will be equal, and the subtraction still yields the correct third value.  

**Complexity**  

* O(1) per test case – only a few arithmetic operations.  
* Memory usage is O(1).  

---

### Plan (step‑by‑step)  

1. **Initialize** reading of integer \(t\) (number of test cases).  
2. **Loop** over each test case:  
   a. Read the line containing the seven integers and store them in a list `b`.  
   b. Assign `a1 = b[0]` (the first element).  
   c. Assign `a2 = b[1]` (the second element).  
   d. Compute `total = b[6]` (the seventh / last element).  
   e. Compute `a3 = total - a1 - a2`.  
   f. Print the three numbers `a1 a2 a3` separated by spaces (order does not matter).  
3. **Terminate** after processing all test cases.  

No additional data structures, sorting, or loops are required beyond the outer test‑case loop, because the input is already sorted and the solution follows directly from the observations above.
**Simulation**

Take the sample input  

```
5
1 3 4 4 5 7 8
1 2 3 4 5 6 7
300000000 300000000 300000000 600000000 600000000 600000000 900000000
1 1 2 999999998 999999999 999999999 1000000000
1 2 2 3 3 4 5
```

Apply the plan to each test case:

1. `b = [1,3,4,4,5,7,8]`  
   - `a1 = b[0] = 1`  
   - `a2 = b[1] = 3`  
   - `total = b[6] = 8`  
   - `a3 = total - a1 - a2 = 8‑1‑3 = 4`  
   → output `1 3 4` (any order is acceptable).

2. `b = [1,2,3,4,5,6,7]`  
   - `a1 = 1`, `a2 = 2`, `total = 7`  
   - `a3 = 7‑1‑2 = 4`  
   → output `1 2 4`.

3. `b = [300000000,300000000,300000000,600000000,600000000,600000000,900000000]`  
   - `a1 = 300000000`, `a2 = 300000000`, `total = 900000000`  
   - `a3 = 900000000‑300000000‑300000000 = 300000000`  
   → output `300000000 300000000 300000000`.

4. `b = [1,1,2,999999998,999999999,999999999,1000000000]`  
   - `a1 = 1`, `a2 = 1`, `total = 1000000000`  
   - `a3 = 1000000000‑1‑1 = 999999998`  
   → output `1 1 999999998`.

5. `b = [1,2,2,3,3,4,5]`  
   - `a1 = 1`, `a2 = 2`, `total = 5`  
   - `a3 = 5‑1‑2 = 2`  
   → output `1 2 2`.

All produced triples satisfy the required condition, and they match the sample output up to permutation (order does not matter).

**Plan Evaluation**

The plan correctly reconstructs a valid array `a` for every test case.

**Result:** **No Need to Modify Plan**.
**Solution Explanation**

For an array `a = [x, y, z]` (all positive) the sums of all non‑empty subsequences are  

```
x , y , z , x+y , x+z , y+z , x+y+z
```

All pairwise sums are larger than each single element, therefore after sorting the
seven numbers we obtain

```
b[0] = smallest of {x, y, z}
b[1] = second smallest of {x, y, z}
b[6] = x + y + z                (the largest sum)
```

Hence the third element of `a` is simply

```
third = b[6] - b[0] - b[1]
```

The three numbers `b[0] , b[1] , third` form a valid array `a`
(the order does not matter).

The algorithm for each test case:

1. read the seven sorted numbers `b`
2. `a1 = b[0]`
3. `a2 = b[1]`
4. `a3 = b[6] - a1 - a2`
5. output `a1 a2 a3`

The input guarantees that a solution exists, so `a3` is always positive.

The algorithm is O(1) per test case and uses O(1) extra memory.

**Reference Implementation (Python 3)**

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        b = [int(next(it)) for _ in range(7)]
        a1 = b[0]
        a2 = b[1]
        a3 = b[6] - a1 - a2
        out_lines.append(f"{a1} {a2} {a3}")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

This program follows exactly the algorithm described above and conforms to the required input‑output format.

### Problem Understanding  

We are given up to 100 independent test cases.  
For each test case we receive a non‑empty string `s` (length 1 … 100, only lowercase letters).  

A string is **square** if it can be written as `xx`, i.e. two identical copies of some non‑empty string placed consecutively.  
Consequently:

* The length of a square string must be even.  
* If the length is even, the first half must be exactly equal to the second half.  

The task: for every test case output **YES** if `s` is square, otherwise **NO**.  
The answer is case‑insensitive, but we will output the canonical upper‑case form.

---

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (Distinct from the current one):**  
*Given a string `t`, determine whether it is a palindrome (reads the same forward and backward).*

**Description**  
A palindrome is a string that equals its reverse. For example, `"radar"` and `"level"` are palindromes, while `"hello"` is not.

**Step‑by‑step Python‑like solution (no actual code)**  

1. Read the input string `t`.  
2. Compute the reversed version of `t`.  
3. Compare `t` with its reversed version.  
4. If they are identical, output `"YES"`; otherwise output `"NO"`.

**Algorithm discussion**  

* The core operation is a linear scan comparing characters from the two ends moving toward the centre, which runs in **O(n)** time where `n` is the length of the string.  
* No extra memory beyond a few variables is required (in‑place comparison).  
* Edge cases: an empty string or a single‑character string are trivially palindromes.

**Planning to solve the palindrome problem**  

1. **Input handling** – read the single line containing the string.  
2. **Two‑pointer technique** – initialise `left = 0`, `right = len(t) - 1`.  
3. While `left < right`, compare `t[left]` and `t[right]`.  
   * If any pair differs, the string is not a palindrome → print `"NO"` and stop.  
   * Otherwise, increment `left`, decrement `right`.  
4. If the loop finishes without mismatches, print `"YES"`.

---

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

The definition of a square string is very simple, which leads to a direct algorithm:

1. **Check length parity** – if `len(s)` is odd, the string cannot be square → answer **NO**.  
2. **Split the string** – let `mid = len(s) // 2`.  
3. **Compare halves** – if `s[:mid] == s[mid:]`, the two halves are identical → answer **YES**; otherwise **NO**.

The algorithm runs in **O(n)** time per test case (where `n` ≤ 100) and uses **O(1)** additional memory.

**Key points to remember**

* A square string must consist of **exactly two** repetitions; more than two repetitions (e.g., `"aaaa"` = `"aa"` + `"aa"` but also `"a"` + `"a"` + `"a"` + `"a"`) are still square because the definition only requires *some* string written twice.  
* The repeated substring may itself be any length ≥ 1, so we only need to test the *half* length of the whole string.  
* Edge cases:  
  * Length 1 → odd → **NO**.  
  * Length 2 → compare the two characters.  
  * Length 100 (maximum) – still trivial for the algorithm.

---

### Plan – Detailed step‑by‑step solution for the original problem  

1. **Read the number of test cases** `t` (integer).  
2. **Loop** over `t` iterations:  
   a. Read the next line as the string `s`.  
   b. Compute `n = len(s)`.  
   c. If `n` is odd → print `"NO"` and continue to the next test case.  
   d. Otherwise (`n` even):  
      i. Compute `mid = n // 2`.  
      ii. Extract the first half `first = s[:mid]` and the second half `second = s[mid:]`.  
      iii. If `first == second` → print `"YES"`.  
      iv. Else → print `"NO"`.  
3. End of program.

**Complexity analysis**  
*Time*: Each test case processes at most 100 characters, so the total time is `O(t * n)` ≤ `O(100 * 100)` = 10⁴ operations – negligible.  
*Memory*: Only a few string slices of size ≤ 50 are stored at any moment → `O(1)` extra space.

**Implementation notes (no code)**  

* Use `input().strip()` to read each string without trailing newline.  
* The comparison `first == second` works directly on Python strings.  
* Output can be accumulated in a list and printed at the end, or printed line‑by‑line inside the loop.  
* Ensure the program respects the case‑insensitivity requirement by outputting uppercase `"YES"` / `"NO"`.

Following this plan will correctly determine whether each provided string is a square string.

**Result:** **No Need to Modify Plan**
```python
def main():
    import sys
    data = sys.stdin.read().splitlines()
    if not data:
        return
    t = int(data[0].strip())
    out = []
    for i in range(1, t + 1):
        s = data[i].strip()
        n = len(s)
        if n % 2 == 1:
            out.append("NO")
        else:
            mid = n // 2
            out.append("YES" if s[:mid] == s[mid:] else "NO")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
```
