### Problem Understanding  

We are given a circular array `a₁ … aₙ` ( `2 ≤ n ≤ 50` ).  
For every neighboring pair we know whether the two numbers are **equal** (`E`) or **different** (`N`).  
The information is supplied as a string `s` of length `n` where  

* `s[i] = 'E'`  →  `a_i = a_{i+1}` (with `a_{n+1}=a₁`)  
* `s[i] = 'N'`  →  `a_i ≠ a_{i+1}`  

The question: does there exist **any** assignment of positive integers to the positions that satisfies all the given relations?  
If it is possible we must answer **YES**, otherwise **NO**.

The crucial observation is that each unordered pair of neighbours appears **exactly once**, except when `n = 2`.  
For `n ≥ 3` the constraints never refer to the same pair twice, therefore they cannot directly contradict each other.  
Even when many “different” constraints form a cycle, we may use as many distinct numbers as we like, so a proper colouring of a cycle is always possible (2 colours for even length, 3 colours for odd length, both are allowed because we have unlimited values).

The only situation where a direct contradiction can arise is when `n = 2`.  
Then the two characters of `s` both describe the *same* unordered pair `{a₁ , a₂}`:

* `s = "EE"` → both say “equal”, consistent.  
* `s = "NN"` → both say “different”, also consistent (choose two different numbers).  
* `s = "EN"` or `s = "NE"` → one says equal, the other says different → impossible.

Hence the answer is **YES** for every test case except the single impossible pattern `n = 2` and `s[0] ≠ s[1]`.

---

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (different from the current one):**  
*Given a string consisting of characters ‘L’ and ‘R’ of length `m`, you have to determine whether it is possible to assign directions to `m` people standing in a line such that each person looks either left (`L`) or right (`R`) and the string describes the relative direction between each adjacent pair (e.g., ‘L’ means the left person looks at the right person, ‘R’ means the opposite).*

**Algorithm Sketch**  

1. Model each person as a vertex in a path graph.  
2. An ‘L’ between `i` and `i+1` forces `a_i = R` and `a_{i+1} = L`.  
3. An ‘R’ forces `a_i = L` and `a_{i+1} = R`.  
4. Propagate these forced assignments from left to right.  
5. If at any step a vertex receives two contradictory assignments → **NO**, otherwise **YES**.

**Step‑by‑step Python‑like Pseudocode**  

```text
read m, s
direction = [None] * (m + 1)          # extra slot for easier indexing
direction[0] = 'L'                    # arbitrary start
for i in range(m):
    if s[i] == 'L':
        # i looks right, i+1 looks left
        if direction[i] is None: direction[i] = 'R'
        elif direction[i] != 'R': impossible
        if direction[i+1] is None: direction[i+1] = 'L'
        elif direction[i+1] != 'L': impossible
    else:  # s[i] == 'R'
        # i looks left, i+1 looks right
        analogous handling
if no contradiction: print("YES") else: print("NO")
```

**Discussion of the Algorithm**  

* The problem reduces to a 2‑SAT‑like propagation on a path, which is linear.  
* Because the graph is a simple path, a single left‑to‑right scan suffices.  
* The key is to detect a vertex that would need to be both ‘L’ and ‘R’ simultaneously.  

**Plan for the Example Problem**  

1. Read input.  
2. Initialise an array `direction` with `None`.  
3. Fix the first person arbitrarily (any value works).  
4. Iterate over the string, applying the forced assignments for each character.  
5. If a conflict appears, output “NO”.  
6. If the loop finishes without conflict, output “YES”.

---

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

1. **Read the test case** – obtain the string `s` and its length `n`.  
2. **Special case `n = 2`**  
   * If `s[0] == s[1]` → answer **YES** (both constraints agree).  
   * Otherwise (`s[0] != s[1]`) → answer **NO** (one says equal, the other says different).  
3. **All other lengths (`n ≥ 3`)**  
   * No pair of neighbours is described twice, therefore the constraints cannot directly contradict.  
   * A construction always exists (e.g., colour the cycle with at most three distinct numbers).  
   * Hence answer **YES**.  

The algorithm runs in **O(1)** per test case (just a length check and a possible character comparison).

**Why it works – short tutorial**  

* For `n ≥ 3` each edge of the cycle is unique, so we never have two opposite statements about the same unordered pair.  
* “Equal” edges simply merge vertices into the same component. After merging, the remaining edges are all “different”.  
* A cycle where every adjacent component must be different is a classic graph‑colouring problem.  
  * Even length → 2‑colourable.  
  * Odd length → needs 3 colours, which we have because integers are unlimited.  
* Therefore a feasible assignment always exists.  

*The only obstruction is when the same unordered pair receives both an `E` and an `N` constraint, which can happen only when the cycle has length 2.*

---

### Plan – step‑by‑step solution for the original problem  

1. **Input handling**  
   * Read integer `t`.  
   * Loop `t` times, reading the string `s` for each test case.  

2. **Process each test case**  
   * Compute `n = len(s)`.  
   * If `n == 2`:  
        * Compare the two characters.  
        * If they are equal → print `"YES"`.  
        * Else → print `"NO"`.  
   * Else (`n >= 3`):  
        * Directly print `"YES"` (no further checks needed).  

3. **Output**  
   * Ensure each answer is on its own line, matching the order of test cases.  

4. **Complexity analysis**  
   * Time: `O(t)` (constant work per test case).  
   * Memory: `O(1)` besides the input string.  

5. **Edge‑case verification**  
   * `n = 2, s = "EE"` → YES.  
   * `n = 2, s = "NN"` → YES.  
   * `n = 2, s = "EN"` or `"NE"` → NO.  
   * Any `n ≥ 3` (including all‑E, all‑N, mixed) → YES.  

Following this plan yields a correct, simple implementation that satisfies the problem constraints.

def solve():

### Problem Understanding  

We are given three integer stick lengths `l1, l2, l3`.  
Exactly **one** of these sticks must be cut into two positive‑integer pieces (the sum of the two pieces equals the original length).  
After the cut we will have **four** sticks.  
The question is: can we arrange those four sticks as the four sides of a rectangle (a square is allowed)?  

For a rectangle we need two pairs of equal lengths:  
- either the four sticks are `a, a, b, b` (with `a` possibly equal to `b`),  
- or after the cut we obtain exactly two sticks of one length and two sticks of another length.  

Thus we must check whether there exists a stick that can be split so that the multiset of the resulting four lengths contains exactly two distinct values, each appearing twice.

---

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (different from the current one):**  
*Given three integers `a, b, c`, determine whether it is possible to reorder them and possibly add a fourth integer `d` (chosen freely) such that the four numbers can be the sides of a rectangle (i.e., two equal pairs).*

**Description**  
We have three known side lengths and we may choose any positive integer for the fourth side. We need to know if we can obtain two equal pairs.  

**Step‑by‑step Python‑like solution (no actual code)**  

1. **Read input** – number of test cases, then three integers per case.  
2. **Sort the three numbers** – let them be `x ≤ y ≤ z`.  
3. **Check three possibilities**:  
   - *Make a pair with the smallest two*: if `x == y`, we can set `d = z` and we already have a pair `x, x`. The rectangle is possible if we can also make a pair with `z` and `d`, i.e. choose `d = z`. This always works, so answer is **YES**.  
   - *Make a pair with the largest two*: if `y == z`, similarly we can set `d = x`.  
   - *Make a pair by splitting the largest*: if `z` is even, we could set `d = z/2` and split `z` into two halves, obtaining `z/2, z/2`. Then we need the other two numbers to be equal (`x == y`).  
4. **If any condition holds → YES, otherwise → NO**.  

**Algorithm discussion**  
The core idea is that a rectangle needs two equal lengths. With three given numbers we either already have a pair, or we can create a pair by splitting the remaining stick (if its length is even). Sorting simplifies the case analysis.  

**Planning for this example problem**  

| Step | Action |
|------|--------|
| 1 | Read `t`. |
| 2 | Loop over test cases, read `l1, l2, l3`. |
| 3 | Sort the three values. |
| 4 | Test the three conditions described above. |
| 5 | Print `"YES"` if any condition is true, else `"NO"`. |

---

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

The original problem is a slight variation: we **must** cut exactly one stick, not optionally. After the cut we must have two equal pairs.  

Key observations  

1. **Four sticks must be of the form** `{a, a, b, b}` (order irrelevant).  
2. Since we cut **exactly one** original stick, the other two sticks stay unchanged.  
3. Therefore the multiset after cutting consists of:  
   - the two untouched sticks (`x` and `y`),  
   - the two pieces obtained from the cut stick (`p` and `q`), with `p + q = original_length` and `p, q ≥ 1`.  

We need to decide whether we can assign values to `p` and `q` (integers) such that the four numbers can be rearranged into two equal pairs.

There are only a few structural possibilities:

| Situation | Reasoning |
|-----------|-----------|
| **Case A** – The two untouched sticks are already equal (`x == y`). Then we need the two pieces from the cut stick to be equal as well (`p == q`). This is possible **iff** the stick we cut has even length (so we can split it into two halves). |
| **Case B** – One untouched stick equals one piece, and the other untouched stick equals the other piece. In other words, after cutting stick `z` we obtain pieces `p = x` and `q = y` (or swapped). This requires `x + y == z` and both `x, y ≥ 1`. |
| **Case C** – One untouched stick equals the other untouched stick after the cut (i.e., we create a new pair from the cut stick, while the two original sticks become the other pair). This is symmetric to Case A and already covered. |
| **Case D** – The two untouched sticks are different, and we want to create a pair from the cut stick while also pairing one of the untouched sticks with one piece. This would lead to three distinct lengths, which cannot form two equal pairs, so it is impossible. |

Thus the only viable conditions are:

1. **Even‑split condition**: There exists a stick whose length is even **and** the other two sticks are equal.  
2. **Sum‑match condition**: There exists a stick whose length equals the sum of the other two sticks.

If either condition holds, answer is **YES**, otherwise **NO**.

**Why these are sufficient**  

- If the even‑split condition holds, cut the even stick into two halves; we now have two equal halves and the two unchanged equal sticks → rectangle.  
- If the sum‑match condition holds, cut the longest stick (or the one that equals the sum) into the two smaller sticks; we now have two copies of each length → rectangle.  

No other arrangement can satisfy the required `{a, a, b, b}` pattern because we are limited to exactly one cut.

**Complexity**  

For each test case we perform a constant amount of arithmetic and comparisons → **O(1)** time, **O(1)** memory.

---

### Plan – Detailed step‑by‑step solution for the original problem  

1. **Input handling**  
   - Read integer `t`.  
   - Loop `t` times, each iteration read three integers `l1, l2, l3`.  

2. **Prepare data**  
   - Store the three lengths in a list `sticks = [l1, l2, l3]`.  
   - Sort the list to obtain `a ≤ b ≤ c`. Sorting makes the two “other sticks” easy to refer to.  

3. **Check the two necessary conditions**  

   **Condition 1 – Even split with equal remaining sticks**  
   - If `a == b` **and** `c` is even → `YES`.  
   - (Because we can cut `c` into `c/2` and `c/2`).  

   **Condition 2 – One stick equals sum of the other two**  
   - If `c == a + b` → `YES`.  
   - (Because we cut `c` into pieces `a` and `b`).  

   *Note*: After sorting, `c` is the largest stick, but the condition is symmetric; we also need to consider the possibility that the even stick is not the largest. However, the even‑split condition only cares that the *other two* sticks are equal, regardless of which stick is even. Therefore we must also test the other permutations:  

   - If `a == c` and `b` is even → `YES`.  
   - If `b == c` and `a` is even → `YES`.  

   This can be done either by checking all three pairs for equality and verifying the remaining stick is even, or by a small loop over the three indices.  

4. **If none of the above checks succeed** → output `NO`.  

5. **Print results**  
   - For each test case, print `"YES"` or `"NO"` on its own line.  

6. **Edge Cases to keep in mind**  
   - All three sticks equal (e.g., `5 5 5`). Here any stick can be split: the other two are equal, and the split stick is even? Not necessarily. If the length is even, condition 1 works; otherwise, condition 2 fails because `5 != 5+5`. So answer depends on parity.  
   - Very small lengths (e.g., `1 1 2`). `2` is even and the other two are equal → YES (split 2 into `1,1`).  
   - Large values up to `10^8` – still fit in 32‑bit signed int, no overflow concerns in Python.  

7. **Complexity verification**  
   - Sorting three numbers is constant time.  
   - All checks are O(1).  
   - Overall O(t) time, O(1) extra memory.  

Following this plan will produce a correct and efficient solution for the problem without writing any actual code in this response.

**Plan Evaluation**

The simulation confirms that the plan correctly handles all sample cases and covers the necessary conditions for constructing a rectangle after breaking one stick.

**Result:** **No Need to Modify Plan**.
**Solution Explanation**

For three sticks `a, b, c` we may break exactly one stick into two positive integer pieces.
After the break we have four sticks and we need to form a rectangle, i.e. we need two pairs of equal
lengths.

Let the lengths after breaking be `x, y, p, q`.  
A rectangle is possible **iff** we can reorder them so that  

```
x = y   and   p = q          (two equal pairs)
```

Because we break only one original stick, the other two original sticks stay unchanged.
Therefore the only ways to obtain two equal pairs are:

1. **Two original sticks are already equal**.  
   Then we must obtain the other equal pair by breaking the remaining stick into two equal
   halves.  
   This is possible exactly when that stick has even length.

2. **One original stick equals the sum of the other two**.  
   Then we break this longest stick into two pieces whose lengths are exactly the other two
   sticks. The four sticks become `{other1, other2, other1, other2}` – a rectangle.

No other situation can give two equal pairs, because we have only one stick that can be split.

Hence the answer is **YES** iff at least one of the following holds:

* there exists an index `i` such that `sticks[i]` is even **and** the other two sticks are equal;
* there exists an index `i` such that `sticks[i]` equals the sum of the other two sticks.

Both checks are O(1).  

---

**Algorithm**
```
for each test case:
    read l[0], l[1], l[2]
    ok = False
    # condition 1: even stick + equal others
    for i in 0..2:
        if l[i] % 2 == 0 and l[(i+1)%3] == l[(i+2)%3]:
            ok = True
    # condition 2: one stick = sum of the other two
    for i in 0..2:
        if l[i] == l[(i+1)%3] + l[(i+2)%3]:
            ok = True
    print "YES" if ok else "NO"
```

**Correctness Proof**

We prove that the algorithm outputs “YES” exactly when it is possible to form a rectangle.

*Lemma 1*  
If two original sticks are equal and the third stick is even, then a rectangle can be built.  

*Proof.* Let the equal sticks have length `d` and the even stick have length `2·e`.  
Break the even stick into two pieces of length `e` each. The four sticks are `{d, d, e, e}` – two
pairs of equal lengths, which form a rectangle. ∎

*Lemma 2*  
If one original stick equals the sum of the other two, then a rectangle can be built.  

*Proof.* Let the longest stick have length `a+b` and the other sticks have lengths `a` and `b`.  
Break the longest stick into pieces `a` and `b`. The resulting multiset is `{a, b, a, b}` – again two
pairs of equal lengths, forming a rectangle. ∎

*Lemma 3*  
If a rectangle can be built after breaking exactly one stick, then either Lemma 1 or Lemma 2
conditions hold.  

*Proof.* After the break we have four sticks that can be paired as `{p, p, q, q}`.
Exactly two of the original sticks were not broken; call them `s1` and `s2`.  
There are two cases:

1. `s1 = s2`. Then the remaining pair `{p, p}` must come from the broken stick, so it was split
   into two equal halves, i.e. its length is even. This matches Lemma 1.

2. `s1 ≠ s2`. Then each of them must belong to a different pair, so the broken stick was split
   into pieces of lengths `s1` and `s2`. Hence its original length equals `s1 + s2`, which is
   Lemma 2. ∎

*Theorem*  
For each test case the algorithm prints “YES” iff it is possible to obtain a rectangle.

*Proof.*  
- If the algorithm prints “YES”, one of the two checked conditions holds.
  By Lemma 1 or Lemma 2 a rectangle can be constructed, so the answer is indeed possible.
- Conversely, assume a rectangle is possible. By Lemma 3 the sticks satisfy either the
  “even + equal others” condition or the “one equals sum of the other two” condition.
  The algorithm checks exactly these conditions, therefore it will set `ok = True` and print
  “YES”. ∎

**Complexity Analysis**

Each test case performs a constant amount of arithmetic and comparisons:  
time = O(1), memory = O(1).  
Overall O(t) time for `t` test cases.

**Reference Implementation (Python 3)**

```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        sticks = [int(next(it)), int(next(it)), int(next(it))]
        ok = False
        # condition 1: even stick and the other two equal
        for i in range(3):
            if sticks[i] % 2 == 0 and sticks[(i + 1) % 3] == sticks[(i + 2) % 3]:
                ok = True
                break
        # condition 2: one stick equals sum of the other two
        if not ok:
            for i in range(3):
                if sticks[i] == sticks[(i + 1) % 3] + sticks[(i + 2) % 3]:
                    ok = True
                    break
        out_lines.append("YES" if ok else "NO")
    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```

The program follows exactly the algorithm proven correct above and conforms to the required
input‑output format.

### Problem Understanding  

We have a rectangular grid of size `n × m` (rows `1..n`, columns `1..m`).  
A robot starts at cell `(r_b , c_b)` and moves each second by a vector `(dr , dc)`.  
Initially `dr = 1` (downwards) and `dc = 1` (rightwards).  

Before each move the robot checks the walls:

* If the next column would be outside `[1 , m]` (i.e. `c + dc` is `0` or `m+1`) the horizontal direction is **reflected**: `dc ← -dc`.
* If the next row would be outside `[1 , n]` (i.e. `r + dr` is `0` or `n+1`) the vertical direction is **reflected**: `dr ← -dr`.

After possibly flipping the signs, the robot moves to `(r+dr , c+dc)` – this consumes one second.

At **every** integer moment (including time `0` before any movement) the robot cleans **all** cells that share its current row **or** its current column.  
There is exactly one dirty cell at `(r_d , c_d)`.  

The task: for each test case, output the smallest non‑negative integer `t` such that at time `t` the robot’s row equals `r_d` **or** its column equals `c_d`.  
It is guaranteed that such a moment always exists.

The constraints are tiny (`n,m ≤ 100`, up to `10⁴` test cases), so a direct simulation is perfectly feasible, but we can also reason analytically. The simplest and safest approach is to simulate the robot step‑by‑step until the condition is met, because the period of the motion is at most `2·(n‑1)·2·(m‑1) ≤ 4·99·99 ≈ 4·10⁴`, far below any time limit.

---

### Recall Example Problem  

**Problem (Bouncing Ball in a Grid)**  

*Statement (different from the current one)*  
A ball moves inside an `n × m` rectangle. It starts at `(x, y)` with velocity `(vx, vy)` where each component is either `+1` or `‑1`. Each second the ball moves to `(x+vx , y+vy)`. If the next position would cross a vertical wall (`x+vx` outside `1..n`) the horizontal component `vx` is negated before the move; similarly for the horizontal walls and `vy`. The ball stops when it reaches a corner cell. Compute the number of seconds needed.

**Algorithm Sketch**  

1. Keep current position `(x, y)` and direction `(vx, vy)`.  
2. Loop:
   * If `x == 1` or `x == n` → `vx = -vx`.  
   * If `y == 1` or `y == m` → `vy = -vy`.  
   * Move: `x += vx ; y += vy`.  
   * Increment a counter.  
   * Stop when `(x, y)` is a corner (`(1,1)`, `(1,m)`, `(n,1)`, `(n,m)`).  

Because the state space `(x, y, vx, vy)` is finite (`n·m·4 ≤ 40 000`), the loop will terminate quickly.

**Discussion of the Algorithm**  

* The simulation directly follows the definition of the motion.  
* Each iteration is `O(1)`.  
* The worst‑case number of iterations equals the size of the state space, which is tiny for the given limits.  
* No need for sophisticated mathematics; correctness follows from exhaustive state traversal.

**Planning to Solve the Example**  

1. Read `t` test cases.  
2. For each case, read `n, m, x, y, vx, vy`.  
3. Initialise a step counter `ans = 0`.  
4. While the current cell is not a corner:  
   * If `x + vx` would be out of bounds → `vx = -vx`.  
   * If `y + vy` would be out of bounds → `vy = -vy`.  
   * Update `x += vx ; y += vy`.  
   * `ans += 1`.  
5. Output `ans`.  

The same pattern (state update → wall reflection → move → check condition) will be reused for the original robot‑cleaner problem.

---

### Algorithm to solve the original problem  

**Core Idea** – *Direct simulation of the robot’s motion*  

The robot’s state consists of four values:

* `r` – current row (1 … n)  
* `c` – current column (1 … m)  
* `dr` – vertical direction (`+1` or `‑1`)  
* `dc` – horizontal direction (`+1` or `‑1`)

At each integer time `t` (starting with `t = 0`):

1. **Cleaning test** – if `r == r_d` **or** `c == c_d`, we have found the answer (`t`).  
2. **Wall reflection** – before moving:
   * If `r + dr` would be `0` or `n+1`, set `dr = -dr`.  
   * If `c + dc` would be `0` or `m+1`, set `dc = -dc`.  
3. **Move** – `r += dr ; c += dc`.  
4. Increment `t` and repeat.

Because the robot’s state space is bounded (`n·m·4 ≤ 40 000`), the loop is guaranteed to terminate quickly, and the problem statement assures that a cleaning moment always occurs.

**Why simulation works and is safe**  

* The robot’s motion is deterministic; each step depends only on the current state.  
* The number of distinct states is finite, so the robot will eventually repeat a state, forming a cycle.  
* Within a cycle the robot visits every row and column that is reachable; the statement guarantees the dirty cell’s row or column is reachable, thus the condition will be met before the cycle repeats.  
* With `n,m ≤ 100` the maximum possible period is `lcm(2·(n‑1), 2·(m‑1)) ≤ 2·99·2·99 ≈ 4·10⁴`. Simulating at most a few ten‑thousands steps per test case is trivial for `t ≤ 10⁴`.

**Alternative (analytical) viewpoint (optional)**  

The robot’s row follows a “bouncing” pattern with period `2·(n‑1)`. Its column follows a similar pattern with period `2·(m‑1)`. The robot cleans the dirty cell when either the row pattern equals `r_d` **or** the column pattern equals `c_d`. One could compute the first time each of those equalities occurs using modular arithmetic, then take the minimum. However, implementing the modular solution correctly is more error‑prone than the straightforward simulation, especially given the tiny constraints.

---

### Plan – Step‑by‑step solution for the original problem  

1. **Input handling**  
   * Read integer `t` – number of test cases.  
   * Loop `t` times, reading six integers `n, m, r_b, c_b, r_d, c_d`.  

2. **Initialize robot state for the current test case**  
   * `r ← r_b`, `c ← c_b`.  
   * `dr ← 1`, `dc ← 1`.  
   * `time ← 0`.  

3. **Main simulation loop** (repeat until the cleaning condition is satisfied)  
   * **Check cleaning condition**:  
     * If `r == r_d` **or** `c == c_d` → output current `time` and break out of the loop for this test case.  
   * **Reflect on walls** (prepare for the next move):  
     * If `r + dr` is `0` **or** `n + 1` → `dr ← -dr`.  
     * If `c + dc` is `0` **or** `m + 1` → `dc ← -dc`.  
   * **Perform the move**:  
     * `r ← r + dr`  
     * `c ← c + dc`  
   * **Increment time**: `time ← time + 1`.  

4. **Output**  
   * After breaking from the loop, print the stored `time` for that test case (one per line).  

5. **Complexity analysis**  
   * Each iteration is `O(1)`.  
   * At most `2·(n‑1)·2·(m‑1) ≤ 4·10⁴` iterations per test case.  
   * Overall time `O(t·n·m)` ≤ `10⁴·10⁴ = 10⁸` worst‑case operations, but the actual bound is far lower; it easily fits within typical limits.  
   * Memory usage is `O(1)` besides input storage.  

6. **Edge cases to verify**  
   * The robot starts already in the same row or column as the dirty cell → answer `0`.  
   * The robot is at a corner; reflections must happen correctly (both directions may flip simultaneously).  
   * Minimum grid size `1×1` or `1×k` / `k×1` – the reflection logic still works because the “next” position would always be out of bounds, causing immediate direction reversal, but the cleaning test will succeed at time `0` if rows/columns match.  

7. **Testing strategy**  
   * Use the sample input provided in the statement.  
   * Additional small manual tests:  
     - `n=m=2, start (1,1), dirty (2,2)` → robot moves to (2,2) in 1 step, but cleans row/col at time 0? No, row 1 ≠ 2 and col 1 ≠ 2, so answer `1`.  
     - `n=3,m=3, start (2,2), dirty (2,3)` → same row at time 0 → answer `0`.  
   * Randomly generate small grids and brute‑force verify that the simulation stops and matches a slower exhaustive search.  

Following this plan will produce a correct, easy‑to‑understand solution for the robot‑cleaner problem using Python 3.

### Plan Evaluation

The simulation confirms that the step‑by‑step plan correctly computes the required time for all sample cases.

**No Need to Modify Plan**.
```python
import sys

def solve() -> None:
    data = sys.stdin.read().strip().split()
    if not data:
        return
    it = iter(data)
    t = int(next(it))
    out_lines = []
    for _ in range(t):
        n = int(next(it))
        m = int(next(it))
        r = int(next(it))
        c = int(next(it))
        rd = int(next(it))
        cd = int(next(it))

        dr, dc = 1, 1
        time = 0
        while True:
            if r == rd or c == cd:
                out_lines.append(str(time))
                break
            # reflect if next move hits a wall
            if not (1 <= r + dr <= n):
                dr = -dr
            if not (1 <= c + dc <= m):
                dc = -dc
            r += dr
            c += dc
            time += 1

    sys.stdout.write("\n".join(out_lines))

if __name__ == "__main__":
    solve()
```
